Preface
This report represents the master’s thesis completed at the Technical University of Den-
mark and is part of the requirements to achieve the MSc of Computer Science and Engi-
neering. The master thesis and all the work associated with it is rated at 35 ETCS point.
The project has been carried out in the period starting from 2016-08-01 to 2017-01-31.
The supervisors Michael Stübert Berger from DTU Department of Photonics Engineering
and Søren Laursen from Altera Denmark Technology Centre A/S have both been provid-
ing guidance in particular with their expertise in hardware design.
The project originated from a student project proposed by Altera Denmark Technology
Centre A/S. The original name was Network Packet Classiﬁer which was changed in the
preliminary stage to describe the project more precisely. The project scope was developed
in more details at the same time. This meant that the purpose was made more abstract
in the sense that the basic objective was to develop an alternative to a content addressable
memory (CAM). The original aim of a Network Packet Classiﬁer still provides one obvious
ﬁeld of application essentially because these classiﬁers often depend upon CAMs.
Special thanks to both my supervisors; Michael Stübert Berger and Søren Laursen for
the guidance and experienced discussions about the project. Many thanks to Anna Jose-
ﬁne Sørensen for the many debates about the subject.
Casper Munch Hansen
February 1, 2017

==PAGE:7==
Abstract
This master’s thesis covers the development of a Content Addressable Memory (CAM)
function realized through the usage of perfect hashing techniques. Many hash algorithms
have been investigated and used as inspiration for the implementation of a hash algorithm
suitable for a Field-Programmable Gate Array (FPGA) chip.
The thesis emerged from a student project described by Altera Denmark Technology
Centre A/S. The developed solution has been compared against a technique made by Al-
tera. These results show that the new technique gives a memory saving of 38% within the
same process time. The solution could even give 53% memory savings within a reasonable
process time interval. The developed algorithm uses a W set of keys which are being
supported by a hash structure. The hash structure relies on the usage of RAM memory.
Results have shown that it can generally be said that a W set with a size larger than 80%
of the available memory addresses will involve an extra ordinary number of attempts.
The ﬁnal solution includes a software program and a Register-Transfer Level (RTL) imple-
mentation. The software part has been implemented as a C++ program and is very easy
to use. It will simply take a W set as input and produce three data ﬁles as output. These
data ﬁles are being used to program the internal hash structure of the RTL component.
The RTL part has been implemented as a VHDL module and has been synthesized for an
Altera Cyclone V FPGA chip. The VHDL module can easily be adapted in any FPGA
design. The ﬁnal VHDL module is very ﬂexible in terms of conﬁguration of ports such
that it can ﬁt many purposes. The case being discussed in this thesis is the packet parsing
unit. These units rely on CAM modules which is why this VHDL component will provide
an alternative to these.

1
Introduction
Many Network Packet Classiﬁers today depend upon content addressable memories (CAMs)
and Ternary CAMs (TCAMs)[17]. The challenge is to develop an FPGA implemented al-
ternative to CAMs that utilizes perfect hashing. It is believed that this can potentially
be used as a substitute to a CAM with the beneﬁt of using a smaller area. The term
area refers to the physical footprint of a design i.e. how much logic, registers, memory
etc. that is used to implement a design. CAMs are used in many applications but it
has been chosen to target a case where the solution must function as a CAM used in the
implementation of a network packet classiﬁer module. This case clariﬁes the usefulness of
the ﬁnal product while it also deﬁnes some requirements to the ﬁnal product.
Previous work and speculations have shown that CAM users and even vendors consider
alternative implementations providing the same features as a CAM[3][26].
The process of packet parsing is described as follows:
Parsing is the process of identifying headers and extracting ﬁelds for pro-
cessing by subsequent stages of the device[17].
This means that packet parsing depends on a header identiﬁcation which locates a header
in an incoming packet. This header will dictate which ﬁelds to extract in the packet. The
header information is not trivial and in some protocols such as OpenFlow it is possible
to update the behavior of a header[27]. This allows updates to change how a packet is
routed through a network which is sometimes referred to as a programmable network.
An simpliﬁed model of a packet parsing module can be seen in Figure 1.1. The control
module identiﬁes the headers and passes this to a CAM which uses this as key and ﬁnds
the matching index. This address is used to lookup associated data in the memory (MEM)
unit. This data will dictate what to parse at the output of the control module.
CAM
MEM
Control module
Search key
Address data
Packet
Match index
Figure 1.1: A simpliﬁed block diagram of a packet parsing module. The control module receives a packet
and parses this. The speciﬁc packet information is stored in a memory (MEM). The content addressable
memory (CAM) is required in order for this information to be located. The CAM modules have been
highlighted in yellow to indicate that it is the intention to improve this. The CAM ﬁnds the search key
location and provides the match index which is the address in the MEM where the associated packet
information is stored. This information can now be used by the control module.

CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
Content Addressable Memory
The invention of the CAM was done by Dr. Dudley Allen Buck (1927–1959). It originated
from his work on building The Cryotron[6] at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology
(MIT). The concept was prepared by him in 1955 and a report describing it was published
in 1956[5]. The term content addressable memory was however not used by himself.
A CAM is a memory that simultaneously checks all the memory locations when a piece of
information is given as input. In other words the content of a speciﬁc memory location is
functioning as the input "lookup-address" while the output is the address for that memory
location[33]. This is the opposite of traditional memory where the input is the address to
the memory location and the output is the content. This is illustrated in Figure 1.2 (a)
and (b) which shows the behavior of a traditional memory and a CAM, respectively. The
CAM performs a lookup and outputs the corresponding address in one cycle which makes
it ideal for applications where speed is crucial such as packet parsing[20].

Figure 1.2: Read operation performed by (a) a traditional memory and (b) by a content addressable
memory (CAM). The traditional memory uses the address location as input and outputs the content of
that address. The CAM does the opposite i.e. it uses a search tag as input and outputs the address with
that content.
CAM and TCAM designs have the advantage of being very fast and entries in the memory
can be updated while in operation.
Furthermore, their width and depth are easy to
conﬁgure. They do however also have some critical drawbacks. These disadvantages are
described in the following:
Expensive: CAMs and TCAMs are very expensive in terms of money. They are generally
avoided due to this fact[4].
High and limiting area consumption: A typical rule of thumb is that the CAM comes
in sizes up to half the largest available size of Static Random-Access Memory
(SRAM). This is due to the fact that one CAM cell is often implemented using
two SRAM cells[33]. The size of a CAM is therefore limited.
==PAGE:13==
1.2. PERFECT HASHING
3
Large power consumption: TCAMs are known to use excessive amounts of power since
a parallel compare is necessary. CAM vendors have however shown to reduce this
problem speciﬁcally for CAMs[3].
A FPGA implementation of a CAM is possible. This will however be rather expensive in
terms of area. The reason for this is that "content addresses" would have to be stored in
registers and cannot use RAM blocks. The is due to the fact that each of these "content
addresses" will have to be accessed. This will require the use of comparators to compare
every single bit. This kind of design would use as many registers as the size of a required
CAM while also requiring the usage of large networks that implements the comparators.
This is why a naive FPGA implementation of a CAM is not desired.
1.2
Perfect Hashing
The word hashing origins from the old French word hache and translates into English as
axe. In English the word hash is often used to describe the action of chopping food such
as vegetables into smaller pieces when cooking. This makes sense in comparison with the
computer scientiﬁc term hashing since it can be described as follows:
Hashing is a process of creating a short digest (i.e. 64 bits) for a message
of arbitrary length, for example 20 Mbytes[34].
Hashing is used today in several applications such as cryptographic[34] or ﬁle synchronization[23].
The latter case comes closest to this application in comparison.
A hash function h(w) maps a set of keys W into a set of integers M[10]. The set of keys
W comes from a Universe U. The principle is illustrated in Figure 1.3. The requirements
for this speciﬁc design is outlined in Section 3.1.
H(w)
Universe U
Keys W
Hash table
Figure 1.3: A simpliﬁed illustration of the main principles of a hash function. The hash function h(w)
maps the set of keys W to a integer in M. The keys comes from a universe U.
A hash function is said to be perfect (i.e. perfect hashing) if there are no collisions[10].
The term collision is deﬁned by Algorithm 1 in Section 3.1 on page 7. Others choose to
deﬁne a hashing technique as perfect hashing if memory accesses can be done in constant
time even in the worst case[14]. Both deﬁnitions are relevant in this project. If the size
of the set W equals the size of the hash table M it is said to be a minimal perfect hash
function. For a more elaborate examination of the basic deﬁnitions please refer to Chapter
1 in [10]. The following quote helps to understand what is considered a good hash function.
A good hash function satisﬁes (approximately) the assumption of simple
uniform hashing: each key is equally likely to hash to any of the m slots,
independently of where any other key has hashed to.[14] Chapter 11.3.
==PAGE:14==
4
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
A hash function can either have a static set of keys or a set of keys that are changeable[14].
A static set of keys allows a programmer to develop one hash function and simply use
this to perform searching. A set of keys that changes requires some additional features
of a hash function. These are: Insert(key), Search(key) and Delete(key)[14]. The search
feature is similar to what is required for a static set. The Insert and Delete is obviously
needed if the set of keys must be changeable. One requirement to this project is that the
set of keys must be changeable hence the Insert and Delete feature must be supported.
This is also being elaborated upon in Section 3.1 on page 7.
1.3
Structure of this Report
This section stated the background for the project while deﬁning the packet parsing case
as an area where the developed solution must be useful. The following Chapter will outline
the set of speciﬁcations provided by Altera Denmark Technology Centre A/S. This set of
speciﬁcations has been predeﬁned before the beginning of the thesis. Chapter 3 states
functional and non-functional requirements which have been formed during the project.
Chapter 4 presents a survey covering several relevant perfect hashing techniques. The
Chapter also presents a categorization of the several algorithms. The categorization was
realized during the thesis to make evaluation of the algorithms easier. The algorithms are
compared and investigated in Chapter 5. This includes a comparison of the algorithms
discussed in Chapter 4. The fundamental theory behind a suitable hashing scheme is also
being covered. The theory is being supported by an experimental evaluation. Finally in
Chapter 5 is the developed algorithm being presented. The choice of algorithm is shortly
being elaborated upon and linked to the presented theory.
The ﬁnal implementation
is being presented in Chapter 6.
This covers the software program and the hardware
component. The implementation is being veriﬁed which is discussed in Chapter 7. The
implementations are used to test the ﬁnal design. These results are presented in Chapter
8. The results are ﬁnally being discussed in Chapter 9 together with an outlook and a
ﬁnal conclusion.
==PAGE:15==
2
Speciﬁcation
The ﬁnal system must be implemented to run on a FPGA. The hardware design must be
made as a VHDL module. This module is named cam_perf_hash. The input interface
to this module can be seen in the block diagram shown in Figure 2.1. The CPU_AW is
a constant which deﬁnes the CPU address width as a positive type. The key width is
also deﬁned by a positive type and named KW. The last constant is the width of the hash
value return which is named IXW and also deﬁned as a positive type. The internal hash
structure must be conﬁgured by a CPU interface. The CPU input signals are all named
as: "cpu_*" and can be seen on Figure 2.1. The CPU interface must be able to perform
writes and reads to two separate hash structures.
The other part of the module is a
key operation interface which must consist of the following signals: a request signal, the
input key and a conﬁguration signal. These are named req_i, key_i and cfg_sel_i.
The request signal is used to request a lookup of the key presented in the key_i input.
The conﬁguration signal dictates which hash structure that should be used to perform a
lookup.
One key operation must be issued in each clock cycle i.e. throughput of 1 key per cycle
is required. This is why two hash structures have to be realized. The module must be
able to make key operation using one hash structure while updating a second structure
not currently in use. This ensures that the key operation throughput can be maintained
at all times.
The outputs are the two CPU signals cpu_rdy_o and a cpu_rddata_o. First signal is
a acknowledge signal which must respond when a CPU instruction has been performed.
If a read is performed will the associated data be presented on cpu_rddata_o. The
rest of the outputs are used by the key operation interface. The rdy_o acknowledges a
key lookup and qualiﬁes the hash output ix_o together with the original key which is
presented simultaneously on match_o.
cam_perf_hash
clk_i : std_logic
srst_i : std_logic
cpu_req_i : std_logic
cpu_addr_i : std_logic_vector(CPU_AW-1 downto 0)
cpu_wr_i : std_logic
cpu_wrdata_i : std_logic_vector(32-1 downto 0)
rdy_o  : std_logic
cfg_sel_i : std_logic
req_i : std_logic
key_i : std_logic_vector(KW-1 downto 0)
cpu_rddata_o  : std_logic_vector(32-1 downto 0)
match_o  : std_logic_vector(KW-1 downto 0)
cpu_rdy_o  : std_logic
ix_o  : std_logic_vector(IXW-1 downto 0)
Figure 2.1: Speciﬁcation of the top module cam_perf_hash.vhd.

3
Requirements
This Chapter discusses the requirements to a ﬁnal solution. The Chapter has been divided
into two section outlining functional and non-functional requirements.
The functional
requirements outline the behavioral features which are required by the ﬁnal solution. The
input and output ports of the hardware component has already been speciﬁed in Chapter
2 which is why it is not being discussed in this Chapter. The functional requirements
consist only of the basic algorithmic deﬁnitions which are being outlined in Section 3.1.
The non-functional requirements are being discussed in Section 3.2. These are addressed
such that a ﬁnal solution can be evaluated based on these. The Section also provides a
reﬂection of the diﬀerence between the desired solution and a CAM component.
3.1
Functional Requirements
This Section outlines the functional requirements. This includes the deﬁnitions of the
algorithms required by the hash structure. The algorithms have been described using
Naive set Theory[19] together with basic algorithm theory[14] in order to formulate them
as short and precise as possible. The notation and deﬁnitions used are similar to what is
found in [14] in Chapter 3. An arrow notion is used to deﬁne a mapping of a set. This is
written as f : A →B which means that f is a function which maps the set A into another
set B.
The ﬁnal solution is meant for use in packet parsing application. This case is used to
deﬁne an input key width. The width of a key therefore matches the width of the address
ﬁeld in a MAC frame. The MAC address has a width of 48 bit [1]. This secures that the
implementation is useful in packet parsing units.
Algorithm 1 - Perfect hash function
The algorithm must deﬁne a function h : W →M that maps the set W
into a set of integers M[10]. Let U be the universe of all usable keys: U =
{0, 1, 2, .., u}, and W be the keys in use such that W is a subset of U: W ⊆U.
The set W consists of n keys: W = {w1, w2, ..., wn} (e.g.
a set of MAC
addresses) where n = |W|. M is the set of available integers: M = {0, .., k−1}
(e.g. memory addresses) where k = |M|.
An integer m ∈M can then be computed when a w ∈W is given, i.e. m =
h(w). A subset of M is denoted by S: S ⊆M.
The function will have to be collision free meaning h(x) ̸= h(y) must apply
for all x, y ∈W when x ̸= y. This implies k ≥n since there must be at least
equal as many integers k in the set M to map each w to a unique m.
Input: A key w from the set W.
Output: The corresponding m ∈M from the computation h(w) = m.
7
==PAGE:18==
8
CHAPTER 3. REQUIREMENTS
Algorithm 2 - Insert key(s) in hash function
The algorithm must be able to insert a new set of keys A from the set U: A ⊆U
into the set W. This requires the function h : W →M to be changeable to
h′ : W ′ →M′ such that A →S is supported, i.e. A ⊆W ′ and S ⊆M′ must
apply after the update. The function h′ : W ′ →M′ must be collision free. The
change of h : W →M is denoted by a prime, i.e. h′ : W ′ →M′. The versions
of h : W →M are from a ﬁnite set H i.e. H →{1, .., p} where p denotes the
maximum of various versions. The keys in A are referred to as being inserted.
A new set of keys A can be rejected if the set results in a violation of the above
requirements.
Input: A new A (set of keys) from the set A ⊆U.
Output: Select new h′ : W ′ →M′ such that A ⊆W and S ⊆M apply. If
a collision free function: h′ : W ′ →M′ cannot be obtained report error
and cancel insertion.
Algorithm 3 - Delete key(s) from hash function
The algorithm must be able to delete an existing set of keys B from the set
W: B ⊆W. This means that the mapping B →S where S ⊆M can no
longer apply for the function h : W →M. The keys in B are referred to as
being deleted.
Input: An existing set B (set of keys) from the set B ⊆W.
Output: Delete mapping of B →S from function h : W →M.
3.2
Non-Functional Requirements
Ideally the hash function should be able to insert any new key x from the universe x ∈U
and also delete any existing key w ∈W. This requires the following to be achieved:
For a function h : W →M, change any existing mapping of w →m where
w ∈W and m ∈M such that a new function h′ : W ′ →M′ is collision free.
This requires an implementation of the function h(w) which can support all
combinations of W →M.
This is known to be possible since the function h : W →M could simply depend on a
lookup table similar to a simple CAM implementation. This mapping can be expressed
as a graph which is shown in Figure 3.1. In graph theory this is referred to as perfect
matching[28]. A bipartite matching of a directed graph is a set of edges chose such that
no two edges share an endpoint. This design is, however, at the expense of high area
cost. The trade-oﬀis to cover as many mapping combinations while keeping the area at
a minimum. The area consumption of a hash function h : W →M covering X mapping
combinations is believed to scale with X.
An upper bound of possible mapping combinations will be the maximum number of
unique mappings. The possible unique mapping combinations of h : W →M when n = 2
==PAGE:19==
3.2. NON-FUNCTIONAL REQUIREMENTS
9

implementation would do. This results in a high area consumption since many nodes are required. The
number of edges equals the number of keys i.e. |E| = n. This is referred to as a perfect matching.
and n = 3 can be seen in Figure 3.2. These are examples of two hash functions each
supporting a W set with two and three keys. It is easy to generalize the calculation of
possible mapping combinations by use of the fundamental counting principle[29].
The
number of combinations C(n) for a given size n can simply be obtained by Equation (3.1)
also known as n-factorial which is deﬁned in Equation (3.2).

It is obviously not feasible to verify that a given hash function supports 4k keys since the
number of combinations required would simply be too high i.e. C(4095). The support of
that many combinations will therefore have to be veriﬁed by a mathematical proof.
Evaluation of Equation (3.1) reveals a very exponential growth of required combinations.
The evaluation of n = {1, 2, ..., 10} can be seen in Table 3.1. This shows that the maximum
of combination scales exponentially hence a solution must allow for this.
There is a process time aspect to the ﬁnal design. A design which uses a small area is
believed to give slower process times of the algorithm outlined in Section 3.1 compared

to a solution using a larger area. This means that a trade-oﬀbetween area and process
time will have to be considered. It has been chosen to favor area since the product is
allowed to use relatively long time on updating keys. The process time is correlated with
the probability of ﬁnding a valid perfect hash function for a W set and is also correlated
to the probability of successively inserting a new key.
There is a trade-oﬀbetween area consumption of the hardware solution and
the process time associated with the execution of Algorithm 1, 2 and 3. A
hardware solution with low area consumption will most-likely provide a low
probability of successively executing Algorithm 1 and 2. This will aﬀectively
give longer process times. The desire for a hardware solution with a small area
will be favored in this context.
The above quote is very much in contrast to a CAM implementation which would be able
to quickly update keys in the trade of much higher area consumption. The ﬁnal solution
is not meant to compete with the key update performance which can be achieved with a
CAM.
One isolated problem is to realize a set of distinct keys W from the universe U. The
universe has |U| unique values while n = |W| can be much smaller. This is referred to as
prehashing. Furthermore is it desired to make a solution where the distribution of the keys
in W does not inﬂuence the behavior of the design. It is not desirable to have a situation
where worst case keys have to be considered. One could consider using knowledge about
the keys. This will, however, aﬀect the versatility of the ﬁnal product which is why no
assumption about the keys W has been included in this work.
==PAGE:21==
4
Survey of Existing Techniques
This Chapter reviews several hashing schemes which are being presented in each of their
own section. Edward A. Fox, Qi Fan Chen, Amhad M. Daoud and Lenwood S. Heath
presents three methods for generation of order preserving minimal hash functions[15].
They outline these methods and chooses to elaborate upon the third method which is
described later in Section 4.4. This study together with the work in [15] has inspired a
categorization of hashing schemes heavily based on these three methods. The following
states this categorization:
Acyclic Graph (ACG) Techniques in this category uses cyclic graphs to generate hash
functions. The techniques require an underlying layer of hash functions which are
used to build a graph. This underlying hash function part is referred to as prehashing.
The prehashing allows edges to be assigned to a hash value in an acyclic graph. The
vertices are thereafter assigned with values. These techniques allow for generation of
hashing functions which are order-preserving. This category is referred to as ACG.
Two Level Hashing (TLH) These techniques are initially using a perfect hash function
to hash each of the keys to a pointer. This step is referred to as level one. This
results in each key being associated with a pointer. Level two constructs a pointer-
mapping which aﬀectively permutes the pointers such that the overall hash function
is order-preserving. This category is referred to as TLH.
Cyclic Graph (CG) These techniques are also graph based as with the ACG methods.
It has been generalized to cover all techniques utilizing a cyclic graph. The method
includes the use of prehashing to build graphs which are allowed to be cyclic. The
majority of techniques in this category will most likely not produce order-preserving
hash function.
There is, however, exception such as the technique discussed in
section 4.4. The category is referred to as CG.
The following reviews aims to categorize the existing technique such that evaluation of
them can be done eﬀectively. Many modern hashing schemes are closely related as they
fall into the same category. It has been the goal to clarify the small diﬀerences between
the investigated techniques. It seems like modern techniques are mostly concerned with
rehashing speed at the expense of area. It is also noticeable that many articles fail to
describe the real hardware area consumption of their algorithm. This has been elaborated
upon in Section 5.1.
The CHM algorithm described in Section 4.2 has been of special interest since the cur-
rent solution used by Altera is based on this. The CHM algorithm is using two universal
hash function to perform the prehashing step. Altera have provided the parametric hash
function used by the prehashing step in their implementation of the CHM algorithm. The
implementation of the parametric hash function is under NDA, but a comparison between
Altera’s technique and the developed hashing method is given in Chapter 8.
The basics of universal hashing is being outlined in Section 4.1. This theory is being
elaborated upon separately since it is used by the majority of the reviewed algorithms.
Speciﬁcally is universal hash function often being assumed to be available and thereby
used to perform the prehashing step. The last Section 4.7 does not describe a hashing al-
gorithm. Instead are several low-level hashing schemes being evaluated. This has inspired
11
==PAGE:22==
12
CHAPTER 4. SURVEY OF EXISTING TECHNIQUES
a technique which makes it possible to design a hardware hash function working similar
to a universal hash function. This is required since many of the algorithms demand such
a function.
The term hashing in algorithmic is often used to deﬁne hashing methods which allows
keys to be updated and is sometimes referred to as dynamic hashing. These kind of hash-
ing schemes can often provide good average case performance [14]. This project does,
however, require a solution which cannot be based on average case performance since a
throughput of one key per cycle must be guaranteed. This requirement makes dynamic
hashing schemes irrelevant to investigate since their theory is not based on worst case per-
formance analysis. This makes popular hashing algorithms such as the cuckoo hashing[32]
irrelevant to this project. A perfect hash function can, however, provide a good worst
case performance when the supported key set is static [14]. Features such as updating and
deletion of keys will therefore have to be emulated with this type of hashing schemes.
4.1
Universal Hashing
This technique is more general theory than it is a speciﬁc method. Many hash functions
can be categorized as universal hash functions.
Understanding the basics of universal
hash functions require the use of probability theory. The distribution of the input keys to
a hash function cannot all ways be guaranteed to be randomly distributed. This means
that the performance analysis of a hash function must consider the distributions of the
keys. Analyzing performance therefore becomes rather comprehensive since worst case,
best case and average case might be relevant to investigate. The average case would be
to assume the keys to be random. This will results in the probability seen in Equation
(4.1). This assumption is called simple uniform hashing since any key w is assumed to be
equally likely to hash into any of the k slots[14]. It is important to emphasize that this
can only be assumed if the keys are randomly distributed for any function h(w). Such
assumptions make hash functions vulnerable to worst case situations. Maybe even more
noticeably is that this is not a reasonable assumption since it is rarely true.
Pw1̸=w2{h(w1) = h(w2)} = 1
k
(4.1)
One method which helps to avoid the problem is to use universal hashing. These types
of functions eliminate the need for someone to put up assumptions about the distribution
of the keys. A universal hash function is chosen randomly and independently of the keys.
This is similar to what is required by function f1 and f2 in Section 4.2 on the facing
page and by many of the other algorithms described. A “key” assumption free solution is
achieved by choosing h(w) randomly from a universal hash family H . This is deﬁned as
follows:
Let h ∈H be a ﬁnite collection of hash functions that map a given universe
U of keys into the range {0, 1, ..., m −1}.
Such a collection is said to be
universal if for each pair of distinct keys k, l ∈U, the number of hash functions
h ∈H for which h(k) = h(l) is at most |H |
m [14] p. 265.
The quote tells that the chance of distinct keys w1 and w2 resulting in a collide will be no
more than 1
k where k = |M| if a hash function h(w) is randomly chosen from the family
==PAGE:23==
4.2. CHM ALGORITHM
13
H . This is the same probability as if the outputs from h(w1) and h(w2) are both chosen
randomly and independently from the set M = {0, ..., k −1}.
Utilization of universal hash functions thereby gives the probability seen in Equation (4.2)
without making any assumptions about the keys from set W. This avoids the need for
making average case analyzes.
Pw1̸=w2{h(w1) = h(w2)} ≤1
k
(4.2)
Equation (4.2) is very similar to Equation (4.1), but it is very diﬀerent to assume random
inputs versus assuming selection of independent and random functions h(w).
4.1.1
An Example
This universal hash function is the one presented in[14]. A hash family is constructed by
ﬁrst selecting a prime number p large enough such that every input key k false into the
range: k ∈{0, 1, ..., p −1}. Secondly is a value m selected such that the range of the
hash function is limited. The universal hash function can be seen in Equation 4.3 where
a ∈{1, 2, ..., p −1} and b ∈{0, 1, ..., p −1}. The p and m values are ﬁxed for this hash
family. A new hash function from the hash family can now be selected by choosing a new
pair of a and b values.
ha,b(k) = ((ak + b) mod p) mod m
(4.3)
4.2
CHM Algorithm
The CHM algorithm was developed by Zbigniew J. Czech, George Havas and Bohdan S.
Majewski (CHM) in 1992[11]. This algorithm falls into the ACG category. They present
a minimal perfect hashing method which depends on three tables, a summation and one
modulo calculation. Their proposed algorithm is order-preserving. The technique does
not consider updates of the hash function as required for this project. It is, however,
possible to emulate this feature with their proposed technique. The article describe their
implemented hash function by the use of a graph G = (V, E). This graph has n edges and
d = c × n vertices, where c is a given constant. Moreover it is required that the graph is
acyclic since this is necessary in order to successfully perform the assignment step which
is explained later. They argue that it is necessary to realize a very sparse graph in order
to obtain a high probability of generating an acyclic graph. This is controlled by the
constant c.
The algorithm is using two functions f1(w) and f2(w) which independently and randomly
maps the set W into a solution space [1, ..., d]. The presented article is speciﬁcally using
two functions which each utilize one table such that letters from the alphabet can be
handled by the functions i.e. through table lookups. Obtaining valid functions f1(w) and
f2(w) is referred to as the mapping step and is illustrated in Figure 4.1 on the next page.
Increasing the constant c will eﬀectively result in a larger d value which will increase the
number of vertices available to the two function: f1(w) and f2(w). This means that the
solution space will be bigger for larger values of d which also results in a larger and more
sparse graph. It is of course desirable to construct two functions allowing a small area
==PAGE:24==
14
CHAPTER 4. SURVEY OF EXISTING TECHNIQUES
consumption which is correlated with d. The graph will have to be build using physical
RAM memory in the FPGA.
The results from the computation of function f1(w) and f2(w) will point to a set of ver-
tices. Each of these is assigned with a value. This is essentially assignment of values to
the vertices in a graph. A function g(x) can thereby be used to lookup the assigned value
of a vertex pointed by the functions f1(w) and f2(w). This can be made such that the
ﬁnal hash function is order-preserving. The ﬁnal hash function can be seen in Equation
(4.4). The function g(x) eﬀectively permutes the results from function f1(w) and f2(w)
such that it matches the order of the set W. This is referred to as the assignment step.
h(w) = (g (f1(w)) + g (f2(w))) mod n
(4.4)
The two functions are thereby used to construct the ﬁnal graph G = (V, E).
This is
done by connecting the resulting vertices {ai, bi} from the calculations f1(wi) = ai and
f2(wi) = bi. This means that keys are represented by an edge i.e. Ewi = {f1(wi), f2(wi)} =
{ai, bi}.
This is done for the whole set W.
After a graph has been constructed the
assignment step can be executed. The assignment step traverses the graph starting at V0
and constructs a function g(x) such that ((g(f1(w1))+g(f2(w1)))modn) = 0, ((g(f1(w2))+
g(f2(w2))) mod n) = 1 and so on. In graph theory this is called a walk from some node ai
or bi to some end node aend or bend[39]. In this case is a trail required since this is a walk
where all edges are distinct[39]. This ﬁnal graph can be seen as a graph where each edge
has been labeled with a key w ∈W. The assignment step can only be done correctly if
G = (V, E) is acyclic. The reason for this is clariﬁed with an example.
Figure 4.1: Graph G = (V, E) illustrates the principles of the CHM algorithm. The w from the set W
is connected to two vertices pointed at by f1(w) and f2(w). These two functions are independently and
randomly mapping W into {0, 1, ..., d −1}. The vertices in each end of every edge between the green and
the blue are assigned with values. This realizes a function g(x) such that their order is preserved when
Equation is computed (4.4). The graph illustrates a speciﬁc connectivity with one speciﬁc assignment of
edges. The number of unique connectivity’s will essentially dictate the possible mappings of W →I. In
the case where n < d will some vertices from f1(w) and f2(w) be left unused.
The principles of the CHM algorithm have been elaborated upon with a small example.
A minimal case for W = {w1, w2} has been chosen. This means that n = 2 which is also
used as the minimum size for the solution space of f1(w) and f2(w), hence d = n. All the
possible results when inserting the keys into function f1(w) and f2(w) can be illustrated
as a graph which is seen in Figure 4.2 on the facing page. It can be seen that 8 acyclic
==PAGE:25==
4.2. CHM ALGORITHM
15
graphs are realizable with labeled edges. Any of these solutions are valid for f1(w) and
f2(w) since their corresponding graphs are acyclic. This ends the mapping step.

Next the assignment step is executed. Essentially this step labels the edges of a graph
such that a speciﬁc order of a set of keys and the corresponding order of the index set M
matches. The step will assign a value to all ai and bi such that edges are being labeled.
This is best explained with an example. It is desirable to construct a function h(w) such
that w1 and w2 are hashed to m1 = 0 and m2 = 1, respectively. This calculation can
be seen in Equation (4.5). Considering the graph from Figure 4.2 (c) it is desirable to
construct the function g(x) as shown in Figure 4.3.
h(w1) = (g(f1(w1)) + g(f2(w1))) mod 2 = 0
h(w2) = (g(f1(w2)) + g(f2(w2))) mod 2 = 1

The construction of g(x) is done by traversing the graph starting at a1.
This begins
by deﬁning g(a1) := 0, next g(b1) := (0 −g(a1)) mod 2 = 0 and ﬁnally g(a2) :=
(1 −g(b1)) mod 2 = 1.
The assignment step cannot be ensured to succeed if the graph includes cycles.
The
mapping step could have constructed the cyclic graphs shown in Figure 4.4. Considering
Figure 4.4 (d) the assignment step starts by deﬁning g(a1) := 0. The next step would be
to assign g(b2) := (0 −g(a1))mod 2 = 0, but now both g(a1) and g(b2) are assigned 0 and
the edge w2 can never be assigned such that h(w2) = (g(f1(w2)) + g(f2(w2))) mod 2 = 1.
This problem is the same for longer cycles containing more vertices.

The drawback of the technique is that the article does not present a deterministic method
to construct the functions f1(w) and f2(w) such that an acyclic graph is constructed. Their
solution is to generate these functions randomly which means that the time to construct
them depends on the probability of ending up with an acyclic graph. The probability for
success increases as the constant d gets higher. This will realize a larger solution space
for f1(w) and f2(w) meaning more nodes can be chosen, which of course gives more pos-
sibilities.
It can be concluded that a sparse graph will be required in order to obtain high proba-
bilities for generating an acyclic graph. The article simply implements f1(w) and f2(w)
as Table 1 and 2 and leave many slots unused. This is also referred to as a direct-address
table[14]. This approach used to construct f1(w) and f2(w) is used such that letters from
the alphabet can be hashed. This is, however, not relevant in the context of this thesis.
These hash functions will therefore have to be replaced by another type of universal hash
functions.

complete initial setup can be seen in 4.5 on the next page (a). The complete process of
realizing a hash function of the form is shown in Equation (4.4) can be seen in Figure 4.5
on the facing page.

edge is labeled with 0 since that was the order of 5 in the set W. This process continues until every element
in W has been assigned to an edge. After this is the graph veriﬁed to be acyclic. The mapping step is
repeated if the graph is not acyclic. This is done by choosing two new Hright and Hleft hash functions.

(c) The Assignment step assigns values to all the used memory slots. This is done by traversing the graph
starting from the left memory following the edge with the lowest labeling i.e. 0. This means that 0 is
assigned to the left memory address 0. Next is the edge labeled 0 followed to the right memory address 0.
The right memory address is also assigned with 0 since Equation (4.4) must result in w = 5 being hashed
to 0. Recall that w = 5 originally generated the edge labeled with 0 hence the edge label is used by the
assignment step. The next edge to follow is the edge labeled with two which goes to the left memory address
3. Once again is Equation (4.4) used to assign the value 2 to the left memory address 3 since it is known
that w = 4 must be hashed to 2. This process continues until the complete graph has been traversed.

4.3
FKS Algorithm
Michael L. Fredman, János Komlós and Endre Szemerédi (FKS) are one of the ﬁrst to
present a method for storage of a sparse table with constant worst case access time.
For speciﬁcs about the algorithm please refer to [16]. They are not using graph theory
even though their design might be representable as a graph. Their contribution was to
prove that construction of a perfect hash function with constant worst case lookup time
is feasible in theory. They are, however, only describing their space storage in O-notation
which makes it hard to evaluate the technique. The technique is also considered outdated
which is why it has not been categorized and is skipped in the evaluations presented in
Chapter 5.
4.4
FCD Algorithm
Edward A. Fox, Qi Fan Chen and Amjad M. Dauod outlines and presents an overview
describing the three basic methods used for generation of order preserving minimal perfect
hash functions. The methods are described in [15]. Their work was used to develop the
categorization described in the introduction of this Chapter.
They choose to describe their third method in more details. This method is of special
interest since it can construct order preserving hash function which uses a cyclic graph.
This technique therefor falls into the CG category while also being order preserving. The
basic idea is to map n keys into a graph G. This graph often includes more than n vertices.
This means that there should be extra storage which can be used to store information
used to preserve the order. This extra storage is utilized through the use of an indirection
method. This allows some keys to be mapped using indirection. Keys subject to the
indirection method are hashed with Equation (4.6) where r is a ratio constant for the h0,
h1 and h2 functions. This leads to 2r being the total number of vertices in the constructed
graph.
h(w) = g ((h0(w) + g (h1(w)) + g (h2(w))) mod 2r)
(4.6)
If a key can be looked up without indirection it is said to be found directly. In this case
the key is found using Equation (4.7).
h(w) = (h0(w) + g (h1(w)) + g (h2(w))) mod n
(4.7)
The g function is essentially the graph G and it is important to notice that it is used for
both direct lookup of a key and for indirection handling. This is how the extra storage in
G is utilized.
They argues that when 2r = n (i.e. the number of vertices equals the key size) will about
13.5 of the vertices have zero-degree meaning that they are left unused. They continues
and states that if these zero-degree vertices can store the order information for a signiﬁ-
cant part of the keys then it is not required that the graph G is acyclic.
A ﬁnal graph G which hold the assigned g function values and mark bits which indi-
cates when to use the indirection hash function seen in Equation (4.6) can be realized
with three steps. The steps are: Mapping, Ordering and Searching this method is gener-
ally used and sometimes referred to as MOS. This concept is the same used by the CHM
algorithm described in Section 4.2.
==PAGE:29==
4.4. FCD ALGORITHM
19
In the mapping step are the three random function h0, h1 and h2 picked such that unique
triples of form {h0(w), h1(w), h2(w)} for all keys are constructed. h1(wi) and h2(wi) de-
ﬁnes connected vertices in the graph G while the edge between them will be labeled with
the order of wi ∈W.
The ordering derives a vertex sequence (V S) and divides the graph G into the following
categories:
C
Connected component in G
AC
C that is acyclic (Single vertices also falls into this category)
CCY C that is cyclic
CP
Maximal subpart of C that is acyclic
CC
CCY −CP resulting in the cyclic subgraph part of C
The vertex sequences generated are categorized as either V SAC or V SCC. These are either
sequences of the vertices in a AC or a sequences of vertices in a CCY . The marking of each
vertex is also constructed in this step. The marking follows a straight forward method for
elaboration upon this please refer to [15].
The ﬁnal step is the searching step which assigns values to the graph. Thereby is the
g function constructed.
The ordering step made this easy by constructing the vertex
sequences. The most critical being the V SCC. This is why vertices belonging to a V SCC
are the ﬁrst to be assigned with an value. These will be involved in the usage of the
indirection Equation 4.6. The rest of the V SAC are assigned with a value at last. The
complete process is restarted if this ﬁnal step fails to construct a valid g function.
4.4.1
An Example
The algorithm has been demonstrated with an example. The example only shows how to
use lookup hash values and does not elaborate upon the construction of the graph G. The
example is taken directly from the article[15]. The graph G with the assigned g values
and mark bits can be seen in Table 4.1. A key can now be hashed by insertion in h0, h1
and h2. The hash functions h1 and h2 will each point to a vertex. The key Clouet will
for example give h1 = 7 and h2 = 12 as seen in Table 4.2. Vertex 7 and 12 is now looked
up in Table 4.1 which shows that their g values are 7 and 6, respectively. The mark bit is
also looked up at the same time these are 1 and 0, respectively. Since the mark bits are
diﬀerent is Equation (4.7) used i.e. the key is directly looked up. This shows that Clouet
is hashed to 3.
Another example is the key: Chungking which gives h1 = 6 and h2 = 14 when inserted in
the ﬁrst hash functions. The mark bits in this case are both 1 meaning that indirection
look up is required hence Equation (4.6) is used. The ﬁnal hash value for Chungking is
thereby 7.

Table 4.2: Example of how keys are being looked up using the three function: h0, h1 and h2. The values
from h1 and h2 are each a pointer to a vertex in Table 4.1. The associated g values and mark bits is
thereby looked up. The keys are directly looked up using Equation (4.7) if the two mark bits are not
equal. Two identical mark bits will indicate that an indirect lookup is required hence Equation (4.6) is
used. It can be seen that the keys are hashed to their speciﬁc order hence the ﬁnal hash function h(w) is
an order-preserving and perfect hash function.
4.5
BMZ Algorithm
This algorithm is developed by Fabiano C. Botelho, Yoshiharu Kohayakawa and Nivio
Ziviani and is described in [7]. This algorithm false into the CG category and does not
construct order preserving hash functions. Since the constructed hash functions are not
order preserving is a second permutation layer required which means that a complete
solution must be categorized as TLH as well.
The method is very similar to the CHM algorithm described in Section 4.2. They generate
random graphs G = (V, E) with |V | = c × n where n equals the number of edges which
corresponds to the number of keys i.e |E| = |W| = n. They allow cycles in the constructed
graph and it is therefore possible to choose a lower constant c compared to the CHM
algorithm.
This allows for a smaller area consumption but this is at the expense of
generating non-order preserving hash functions. The complete hash function can be seen
in Equation (4.8) which is quite similar to Equation (4.4) presented in Section 4.2.
h(w) = g (h1(w)) + g (h2(w))
(4.8)
Their method follows a MOS approach. The mapping step builds the graph G by selecting
two function h1 and h2 which are both universal hash functions. Each function h1(w) and
h2(w) points at a vertex and the edge is given by: ew = {h1(w), h2(w)}. The presented
technique does not use a bipartite graph rather is a complete graph used. This means
that self-loops can be generated if both h1 and h2 points to the same vertex i.e. h1 = h2.
This is still not allowed and is solved in an ad hoc manner by the use of Equation (4.9)
where u is the size of the universe U.
h2(w) = (2h1(w) + 1) mod (u −1)
(4.9)
Equation (4.9) is used until a loop is no longer generated. The run is aborted and the
complete process restarted if multiple edges between vertices are generated. The restart
will result in a new selection of h1 and h2 is executed. Generation of suitable h1 and h2
functions ends the mapping step. The algorithm allows cycles in the graph and is therefore
==PAGE:31==
4.5. BMZ ALGORITHM
21
divided into subcomponents similar to the approach used by the FCD algorithm presented
in Section 4.4. This is done in the ordering step. The subcomponents in the graph are
categorized as seen below:
Gncrit Acyclic subgraph of G
Gcrit
Cyclic subgraph of G
G
Complete graph i.e G = Gcrit ∪Gncrit
The Gcrit subgraph is found by iteratively removing vertices with degree one until no more
are present. This will result in only the vertices which are part of a cycle being left. The
removed vertices will be the ones belonging to the Gncrit subgraph. This ends the ordering
step.
The last step is the searching step which will assign g values to each vertex such that the
usage of Equation (4.8) will generate a unique number for each edge i.e. for each key. The
ﬁrst vertices that is assigned with an g values is the ones in the Gcrit subgraph. This part is
processed ﬁrst such that reassignments are resolved early in the process. The assignment
is performed with a breadth-ﬁrst search and each vertex is simply assigned with a g values
as they are discovered by the search. Let’s call the assigned value for x hence assigning
a g value is done by: g(v) = x. Recall that keys are looked up by summing the assigned
g values of two vertices connected by an edge. This means that assigning a x which will
result in two edges with the same sum is not allowed. In this case is another x value for
g(v) being tried. Speciﬁcally is x + 1 tried which is referred to as a reassignment. The
reason why two edges resulting in the same sum is not allowed can be found in Equation
4.8 which clarify that this would result in two keys hashing to the same value. This would
obviously result in two edges which cannot be distinct from each other. This ﬁnishes the
assignment of g values for the Gcrit subgraph.
The second part is the assignment of g values to the Gncrit subgraph. This is trivial since
no reassignments are necessary. The assigned g values can simply be assigned such that
the rest of the hash addresses are being used. This is done with a depth-ﬁrst search. It is
important to notices that the search is started from a vertex belonging to Gcrit since this
already has a g value assigned and cannot be changed.
4.5.1

in the presented article [7]. The mapping step generated a random complete graph which
can be seen in Figure 4.6 (a). The ordering step divides the graph G into subcomponents:
Gcrit and Gncrit. This can be seen in Figure 4.6 (b). This is done by iteratively removing
vertices with degree 1. The eﬀect of this is that vertex 1 and 2 has degree 0 after the
process.
This is however only a manipulation of the degree ﬁeld.
The graph is now

4.6
BDZ Algorithm
This algorithm is made by Fabiano C. Botelho, Rasmus Pagh and Nivio Ziviani [8]. The
proposed algorithm is heavily based upon the technique developed by Bohdan S. Majewski,
==PAGE:33==
4.6. BDZ ALGORITHM
23
Nicholas C. Wormald, George Havas and Zbigniew J. Czech [25]. The algorithm uses the
concept of generating an acyclic graph and it is therefore categorized as ACG while the
ﬁnal hash function is not order-preserving. The method associates input keys with edges
in a graph similar to both the CHM and BMZ algorithm described in Section 4.2 and
4.5, respectively. One big diﬀerence is that they map these keys into a hypergraph. They
thereby present a family of minimal perfect hash functions which is realized through the
construction of a r-uniform hypergraph.
A hypergraph is a graph where an edge can
connect any number of vertices. One edge connecting only one vertex is also a valid edge.
The concept of hypergraph can be seen in Figure 4.8. The graph in the algorithm is a
2
1
3
4
5
6
7
1
Figure 4.8: The concept of hypergraphs. A graph has a set of vertices: V which can be connected by
edges in the set E. A vertex has been drawn as a yellow box to indicate that it will be implemented as an
address in a memory while edges has been shown as colored circles around the connected set of vertices.
r-uniform hypergraph which means that each edge is connecting r vertices. The value can
be set to any value higher than 2 since at least two vertices has to be connected. The
2-uniform and 3-uniform hypergraphs has however shown to be of special interest since
these generates the most compact and eﬃcient functions. This is why they are being
speciﬁcally evaluated in the article [8].
The algorithm uses the concept of three steps: mapping step, assigning step and a Ranking
step. The mapping steps creates a acyclic random hypergraph Gr = Gr(h0, h1, ..., hr−1)
together with a list of edges L.
The authors assumes that they have universal hash
functions at their disposal. The mapping step builds a graph such that the hash function
in Equation (4.11) can be used where i indicates the selection of a universal hash function
from the r family: hi : U →[im

r ], 0 ≤i < r. A key w can be looked up using
either of the universal hash functions: h0(w), h1(w) to hr−1(w). Which one to use is
selected by Equation 4.10 which will compute a i value which is used to select one of the
hash functions from the r family. This means that Equation 4.11 is used when a i has
been computed.
i(w) = (g(h0(w)) + g(h1(w)) + ... + g(hr−1(w)) mod r
(4.10)

where k = |M|. The ﬁnal graph constructed will be a r-partite graph since edges will con-
nect r subparts of the graph and no edge in a subpart will ever exist.

Lastly in the mapping step is the list L of edges ordered such that each edge ei contains
a vertex that is not to be found in any e that comes after ei in L. This ends the mapping
step.
The assignment step assigns g values to all vertices in the graph Gr such that g : V (Gr →
{0, 1, ..., r −1}. This is done by traversing the vertices in the graph Gr which is done in
the reverse order of the edge list L e.i. from ek to e0. This traversing order ensures that at
least one vertex is traversed for the ﬁrst time. Each time a vertex is visited it is assigned
with a g value and marked as visited = true. The complete procedure works as follows.
For each edge e ∈L (in reverse order) is the ﬁrst vertex u belonging to e being visited if
it has not yet been visited. The g value is assigned with Equation (4.12) where j is the
index of u in e hence j = {0 ≤j ≤r −1}. This ends the assignment step.
g[u] =

(4.12)
The assignment step only builds a perfect hashing function (PHF) for the W set. This
means that a PHF with k = |V (Gr)| where n ≥k will be built if this step is omitted i.e
p(w) will be a PHF. The PHF can however be mapped into a smaller range such that a
minimal perfect hash function (MPHF) is constructed i.e. n = k. This is realized by the
last step which is the ranking step. This constructs the ﬁnal MPHF which can be seen
in Equation (4.13) for a given r value. The rank function maps the vertices in the graph
such that: V (Gr) →[0, n −1]. This makes it possible to realize the ﬁnal MPHF.
h(w) = rank(p(w))
(4.13)
Evaluation of the rank function seen in Equation (4.13) is done as follows. Let u be the
input in rank(u) this will result in a lookup in a rankTable. The rankTable consists of
precomputed indices for some v ∈V . A lookup is performed with a selection of the largest
v closest to u such that v ≤u−1. The ﬁnal hash is thereby given by counting the assigned
vertices from v to u −1.

i(w) = (g(h0(w)) + g(h1(w)) + g(h2(w)) mod 3

4.7
Comparison of Hashing Schemes
This section outlines the important ﬁndings in a very short survey done by Jain R [21]. His
work is of special interest since the hashing schemes evaluated is targeted address lookup
in computer networks. Moreover most of the presented hashing schemes can readily be
implemented in IC design. The survey does not evaluate methods which are based on
graph. The many hashing schemes all falls into the TLH category since they are very
basic hashing methods. None of them will realize an order-preserving hash function. The
schemes can instead be considered useful for performing the ﬁrst level of the TLH methods.
The article presents a framework used to benchmark the evaluated hashing schemes. A
hash function is being deﬁned as a function which takes an address and maps this into
a cell i in a array with size M. Each cell will point to the start of a sub-table with ni
entries. This sub-table is searched through when a lookup is performed. The ﬁnal goal is
to minimize the average number of lookups required per frame. In order to measure this
is a ri value being deﬁned as the number of frames (input addresses) that hashes to the
same i’th cell in the cell array. Thereby is Equation 4.16 used to evaluate the information
content of a hash function which is measured in "bits". This is also referred to as the
entropy of the hash function[30].
Information contentbits =
X 
−ri
R log2
ni
N

,
where R =
X
ri, N =
X
ni. (4.16)
Several hashing schemes has been benchmarked using Equation 4.16. The hashing schemes
tested are the following:
Address bits
CRC
Fletcher checksum
Mod-chechsum
==PAGE:36==
26
CHAPTER 4. SURVEY OF EXISTING TECHNIQUES
Xor folding
The ﬁrst method does nothing else than extracting m bits from the input address. This
does not require any computation when an evaluation is required. The technique is not
providing much information in bits when evaluated with Equation 4.16. The bits in the
ﬁrst 3 octets are especially low on information.
The reason for this can be found in
the IEEE 802 standard [1]. These bits are being assigned by the IEEE which is why
most stations have the same bits hence resulting in low information. The ﬁfth octet is
determined to provide the most information on lookup.
The utilization of a CRC polynomial is more promising. The author is using the 32 bit
CRC polynomial deﬁned by the IEEE 802 standard [1]. This shows that all 32 bits have
high information content. They conclude that a CRC provides an almost optimal hashing
function. An 8 bit and a 16 bit CRC polynomial were also analyzed. The whole CRC
polynomial analysis resulted in the following statement:
In general, we found that if a polynomial provides a good CRC, it can serve
as an excellent hash function.[21].
The Fletcher Checksum technique is investigated since it easy to compute in software.
The technique is being said to provide a good hashing function. For the details about the
algorithm please refer to [21].
The mod-checksum is deﬁned in Equation 4.17 where b[i] is the i’th octet of the input
address and C is the 16 bit checksum.
C =

28 (4b[1] + 2b[3] + b[5]) + (4b[2] + 2b[4] + b[6])

mod 216 −1.
(4.17)
It was found that this technique was not as good as the Fletcher Checksum when the
information content was compared.
The ﬁnal technique was the Xor folding. This has been deﬁned in Equation 4.18 which
shows that the 6 octets are being xor’ed. The C output will be a vector of 8 bits. They
conclude that this simple function also works as an excellent hash function.
C = b[1] ⊕b[2] ⊕b[3] ⊕b[4] ⊕b[5] ⊕b[6]
(4.18)
The ﬁnal verdict is that the CRC polynomial method serves as an excellent hashing func-
tion while the Fletcher’s checksum and the xor-folding techniques are also working as good
hashing functions. The mod-checksum should not be considered since it is outperformed
by the Fletcher’s checksum and is more complex to compute.
==PAGE:37==
5
Designing the Algorithm
The survey presented in Chapter 4 has been evaluated and used to develop the ﬁnal al-
gorithm. The ﬁnal algorithm is not directly an implementation of any of the evaluated
techniques. It is mostly inspired by the CHM algorithm. This choice is being elaborated
upon in Section 5.1. The CHM was categorized as an ACG method. This ACG approach
is being analyzed in Section 5.2 in order to investigate the best choice of graph architec-
ture.
A universal hash function was required by the CHM algorithm. A hardware friendly hash-
ing technique based on the theory behind the implementation of a CRC polynomial has
been developed and discussed in Section 5.3. The results from a practical evaluation of
the performance impact in regards to the graph structures and the CRC hashing tech-
nique are being presented in Section 5.4. These results were realized through a developed
framework which is outlined in Section 5.6. The framework depends on the generation of
test data. Details concerning the test data are outlined in Section 5.5.
The developed algorithm is ﬁnally discussed and presented in Section 5.7.
5.1
Comparison of Existing Techniques
The analysis done by the authors of the evaluated algorithms are mostly done with O-
notation. This only states how well an algorithm scales compared to the size of a problem.
This also means that the analysis is done with asymptotic notation resulting in constant
factors being left out of performance analyzes. It is therefore hard to base any conclusion
on these analyses.
For a detailed explanation please refer to Chapter 3 in [14] which
also elaborates upon some common abuses. It has therefore been necessary to decide on
a parameter which would make it possible to quickly compare the evaluated techniques
from Chapter 4.
The majority of the algorithms described in Chapter 4 were based on the construction of
a graph. The area of the constructed graph has been chosen as the comparison parameter
in order to cope with the need for a comparison method. This is considered a fair choice
since the graph will have to be stored in a RAM which is why the size of the graph is of
special interest.
Many of the authors of the algorithms from Chapter 4 are concerned with the number of
vertices a constructed graph is requiring. This has helped to establish comparable data
which is shown with a plot and a table seen in Figure 5.1. These have been constructed
with the use of the MATLAB script found in Appendix A.1.1. It is important to state
that the equations which are being used to compute these results come from the authors
of the evaluated algorithms themselves. This is with the exception of the CHM algorithm
as authors behind the technique did not report any graph size. Instead this estimation is
based on the work done by Fabiano C. Botelho, Yoshiharu Kohayakawa and Nivio Ziviani
[7].
The BMZ and BDZ algorithms are not building order-preserving hash functions.
These solutions will therefore require an extension which allow the outputs to be permuted
such that an order-preserving implementation is established. Permutation of such a set
can be done with a RAM. The size of a permutation RAM is therefore added to their
original graph size in order to make a fair comparison. A permutation RAM with the size
n = |W| would make it possible to permute the keys such that the ﬁnal hash would be
27
==PAGE:38==
28
CHAPTER 5. DESIGNING THE ALGORITHM
order preserving. This expression is therefore added to the original formula for computing
graph sizes. Later in Section 5.4 it is shown through experimental evaluation that graphs
with much smaller sizes than the ones presented here can support much larger sets. The
theory supports these ﬁndings and is being elaborated upon in Section 5.2.
The analysis presented in this Chapter does, however, provide a very good and quick
approach for performing fair area comparison of hashing schemes based on graphs.

Figure 5.1: A quick estimation of the area required by the graphs built by the following four graph-
based hashing algorithms: CHM, FCD, BMZ and BDZ. (a) shows the plotted results. (b) reveals the
precise size of a graph which support the two key set sizes: 2000 and 4000. The estimations are based
on the work done by the authors of the algorithms themselves, except for the CHM estimation which is
based on the work presented in [7]. A constant factor n = |W| has been added to the original graph
size estimations of the BMZ and BDZ algorithms in order to make a fair comparison since these will now
realize an order-preserving hash algorithm.
The FCD algorithm outperforms the other techniques according to Figure 5.1. The reason
is that the FCD is the only technique utilizing a cyclic graph while still realizing an order
preserving hash function. The basic of the technique does however demand that a lookup
can result in a second lookup in the same data structure. This was referred to as an
indirection lookup in Section 4.4. This will eﬀectively require a hardware solution which
must allow access to the same address space of a RAM, after an initial lookup in that RAM
has already been performed. This will demand a copy of the RAM in order to maintain
a throughput of one key lookup per cycle. This extra area has not been considered in the
area estimation shown in Figure 5.1. This is why the FCD algorithm seems to outperform
the other techniques.
A ﬁnal solution which maintains the required throughput will,
however, double the estimated sizes since the physical RAM block will have to be copied.
The BMZ and BDZ algorithms are heavily aﬀected by the required permutation RAM.
This shows that it is very unlikely that a non-order preserving perfect hashing scheme will
be able to compete with one that is order-preserving. This leaves the CHM algorithm as
the best choice. This technique furthermore beneﬁts from being very simple to implement.
==PAGE:39==
5.2. THEORY BEHIND GRAPH STRUCTURES
29
5.2
Theory Behind Graph Structures
Section 5.1 indicated that the CHM algorithm had the lowest area consumption when a
throughput of one key per cycle was required. The fundamental technique was categorized
as an ACG method while the CHM approach is speciﬁcally making use of an acyclic
bipartite graph. This section investigates how the choice of graph structure aﬀects the
time it takes to realize a valid ACG. This process was referred to as the mapping step in
Section 4.2. More speciﬁcally the following types of graph structures are being evaluated:
complete-, bipartite- and m-partite graph. This work carried out in this thesis deﬁnes a
complete graph as a undirected graph where edges between all vertices are allowed i.e. an
edge from va to va is allowed and is referred to as a self-loop. A bipartite graph is deﬁned
as a undirected graph divided into two subpart of vertices. Edges between subparts are
the only ones allowed. The last category of graph structures are the m-partite graphs.
These are similar to the bipartite types except for being divided into m-subparts. The
bipartite graph can also be described as a 2-partite graph where m = 2.
The individual keys in the set W is directly mapped to one speciﬁc edge in a graph. This
means that 4000 edges would be required if |W| = 4000. This makes it important to have
a design which allows many edges to be constructed. Recall that the CHM technique
used two universal hash functions: Hleft and Hright to select a pair of vertices that would
then be connected by an edge. Each edge was then associated with a unique key. It is
trivial that a complete graph would allow the most edge conﬁgurations to be generated.
It will also be the graph structure where the highest number of edges can exists. The
CHM algorithm uses a bipartite graph to avoid the generation of self-loops. They argue
that this improves rehashing performance. This choice does, however, limit the number
of edges signiﬁcantly. This case is in fact the structure which allows the lowest number of
edges in a graph.
This outlines two extremes which are the complete graph structure and the bipartite graph
structure. There are several structures in-between these two extremes. These are the ones
called m-partite graph structures.
The maximum number of edges in a graph with |V | vertices has to be computed before
the number of possible edge conﬁgurations can be determined. Figure 5.2 outlines the
basic calculations which determine the number of edges in a graph. Figure 5.2 (c) is the
one used since this considers undirected graphs. The computation seen in Figure 5.2 (c)
is not considering self-loops since this scenario will construct cyclic graphs.
(a) Complete directed graph al-
lowing self-loops.
(b) Complete directed graph dis-
allowing self-loops.
(c) Complete undirected graph.

(b) are shown only to clarify how the equation seen in (c) has been derived. The work carried out in this
thesis only considers undirected graphs. The graph in (c) could be modiﬁed to include self-loops which
would simply add a constant of |V | in the equation shown.

These two functions have been used to investigate how many edge conﬁgurations that are
possible for several graph types. The tested graph structures are: complete, 16-partite,
8-partite, 4-partite and bipartite. The investigation correlates the total number of edge
conﬁgurations with the size of the physical memory used to store the graph which is
essentially the number of vertices V . This is due to the fact that each vertex v will occupy
one memory address. The comparison can be seen in Figure 5.3. It can be seen that the
bipartite structure leads to the lowest number of edge conﬁgurations. The complete graph
structure is the one allowing the highest number of edge conﬁgurations. The analysis was
executed with MATLAB and the source code can be found in Appendix A.1.2.
The theory shows that an ACG method which utilizes a complete graph will provide more
valid conﬁgurations as more edges are allowed. The drawback with the complete graph
is that this would allow for self-loops to be generated which might slow down rehash
performance. The rehash performance is the time it would take to compute a valid ACG
solution. The performance might be aﬀected since a higher number of edge conﬁgurations
would result in a cyclic graph which would require a rehash. The performance impact is
being investigated in Section 5.4. The construction of a m-partite graph structure with
m > 2 will require additional two hash functions to select between the subparts. These
solutions will therefore most likely result in a high decrease of the performance. This is
elaborated upon in Section 5.6. The determination of a lower bound for the size of the
acyclic graph is discussed in Section 5.2.1.
5.2.1
ACG Lower Bound
As stated in Section 5.2 will the size of |W| deﬁne how many edges that is required in a
graph. Another requirement is that the graph has to be acyclic. The lower bound for the
graph size will be a case where exactly |W| edges can exists while maintaining an acyclic
graph. This type of graph are referred to as a spanning tree in graph theory [39]. A
spanning tree is a graph where every vertex is connected while containing no cycles. This
means that adding any additional edge will construct a cyclic graph and removing one will
result in two disjoint sets of the graph. This can be deﬁned by a mathematical proof which
conﬁrms that a speciﬁed number of key combinations can be supported. Determination
of this was deﬁned as a non-functional requirement in Section 3.2. The theorem that

outlines the theory behind a spanning tree is being deﬁned here due to its importance to
the solution. For information about the proof please refer to theorem 9.1 in [39].
Theorem 9.1[39] Let G be a graph with n vertices. Then the following statements are
equivalent:
(i) G is a tree;
(ii) G contains no cycles, and hash n −1 edges;
(iii) G is connected, and has n −1 edges;
(iv) G is connected, and each edge is a bridge;
(v) any two vertices of G are connected by exactly one path;
(vi) G contains no cycles, but the addition of any new edge creates exactly one
cycle.
Theorem 9.1 can be used to deﬁne a maximum set of edges that can exist in an acyclic
graph since it outlines how a spanning tree is deﬁned. This is used to deﬁne a lower bound
for the graph size. A fully connected graph with 4 vertices can be seen in Figure 5.4 (a).
All the spanning threes in that graph is shown in Figure 5.4.

theorem 9.1. Equation (5.3) deﬁnes the formula. The number of unique spanning trees can
be calculated using Cayley’s theorem [39] which can be seen in Equation (5.4). Using this
formula on a graph with 4 vertices gives: Edge conﬁgurations in Spanning Treeconﬁgs(4) =
16 which conﬁrms the results seen in Figure 5.4 (b).

generates. The evaluation has been plotted and can be seen in Figure 5.5 (a). The data
for a complete graph shows a very fast growth of available edges when the number of
vertices increases. The same fast growth is present for the graph when only spanning tree
edges are considered. This growth does, however, saturate at a much earlier stage. The
analysis also reveals that a set |W| = 4000 would require a spanning tree with V = 4001
vertices while the same set can theoretically be supported by a complete cyclic graph with
V = 90 which would actually support a set of |W| = 4005. This shows that there is a
huge diﬀerence in how many edges that can exist in a complete graph setup versus a graph
setup only allowing spanning trees. This indicates that a high area decrease potential is
present for a solution which falls into the CG category. The solution closest to match
these requirements is the FCD algorithm. The drawback implicated with this approach
was outlined in Section 5.1.

ble edge conﬁguration of the complete graph setup compared to the graph setup allowing
only spanning trees. The number of vertices has been limited such that it ranges from 0
to 130. This limited range is due to the fact that the number of edges quickly increases
to a very high number. The tendency can, however, still be seen. The complete analysis
was done with MATLAB and the source code can be seen Appendix A.1.2.

5.3
A Hash Family of CRC Polynomials
The important ﬁndings by Jain R [21] was elaborated upon in Section 4.7. The article
investigated several hashing schemes. The usage of a CRC polynomial was said to be
especially good as a hashing function.
A CRC polynomial is readily implemented in
IC designs [9]. It has therefore been chosen to implement a hash function based on a
CRC polynomial. The ACG hashing methods depended upon a family of universal hash
functions. A family of hash functions makes it possible to select another pair: Hleft and
Hright if the current set were causing a failure when executing the mapping step. This is
why a CRC polynomial with a programmable set of coeﬃcients is required. This would
eﬀectively give a conﬁgurable CRC. Conﬁguring the CRC would be the same as selecting
another hash function from the family. In that way is a hash family of CRC polynomials
being constructed easily in IC design.
Many articles describe the idea of a programmable CRC [18, 37, 38]. A trivial hardware
solution has, however, been chosen in order to construct the CRC. The maximum degree
of the polynomial will have to match the bit size of the input key: wbits. The output from
the hash will have to match the number of available vertices in the graph such that every
vertex can be addressed. This address space is deﬁned from 0 to a value P −1. The bit
size of this number is denoted with Pbits. Implementation of the CRC polynomial will be
easiest if P is set to any power of 2 number. The reason for this is that such a size would
perfectly match the address space of a RAM. This work only considers input keys with
bit size = 48 since this is the width speciﬁed in the Functional Requirements outlined
in Section 3.1. The concept of a programmable CRC can be seen in Figure 5.6. This
shows a Galois Linear Feedback Shift Register (LFSR) implementation of the CRC [23].
==PAGE:44==
34
CHAPTER 5. DESIGNING THE ALGORITHM
Another version named Fibonacci LFSR also exists. The shown design will, however, only
do bit-serial computation of CRC values.

FF
...

FF
Figure 5.6: A Galois Linear Feedback Shift Register (LFSR) implementation of a bit-serial CRC. It
shows the concept of a programmable CRC polynomial. The g constants allows setup of the CRC and is
thereby programmable through the use of the g constants.

behind construction of a parallel version of the CRC[18, 9].

The transmitted data stream T(X) from a CRC can be described with Equation 5.8
where F(X) is an identity matrix. The identity matrix F(X) is raised to the power of
wbits in order to determine the T(X) value after wbits cycles. This makes it possible to
compute the CRC of a 48 bit MAC address in one cycle. This means that the prehashing
step can be done in just one cycle. It is important to mention that the design can be
relaxed by allowing the CRC to be computed in two cycles or more. Such a technique will
have to consider reminders in the construction of a generator matrix. These reminders
are discussed in a moment. The identity matrix F can be seen in Equation (5.9) where
[c0, c1, ..., cP−1] is the coeﬃcients of a chosen CRC polynomial. It is only these coeﬃcients
that are being changed when a new CRC hash function is selected. This makes generation
of a new CRC hash very easy. Computation of F 48 is also relatively easy since it is a
square matrix. This means that the calculation will not change the dimensions of the
matrix. The generator matrix gP−1,47 can after the power calculation be constructed by:
gP−1,47 = F[P −1 : end, 0 : 47] which is the highlighted (in blue) part of Equation 5.9.

5.4
Experimental Evaluation
Several experiments have been carried out in order to investigate all the characteristics of
a ﬁnal hash function. The ﬁrst test evaluates how the choice of graph architecture has an
impact on performance. This has been done by construction of a framework which has been
realized as a C++ program. This is referred to as ACG framework since it evaluates only
Acyclic Graph (ACG) architectures. A detailed description of the ﬁnal ACG evaluation
framework can be found in Section 5.6. The implemented C++ program can be found in
==PAGE:46==
36
CHAPTER 5. DESIGNING THE ALGORITHM
Appendix C.1. The C++ program was set to run 10.000 evaluations of a design with 5000
vertices witch would occupy 5000 physical memory addresses. Moreover has a limit of
40.000 attempts for each evaluation been set in order to limit the total computation time.
Thereby has the following graph structures been evaluated: complete, bipartite, 4-partite,
8-partite and 16-partites. The outcome has been processed with the two MATLAB scripts
found in Appendix A.1.3 and A.1.4. The results from the m-partite graph structures with
m > 2 has not been satisfying since they showed to be very computation heavy. This
meant that the limit of 40.000 attempts was often met. The required amount of data
could not be establish in order to make a good analysis because of this.
The experimental results from evaluation of a complete- and a bipartite graph structure
are presented in Figure 5.8. These results has been carried out with the use of the universal
hash function described in the example in Section 4.1.1. This was chosen to provide the
best conditions for the evaluation of the ACG methods.
