Another comparison was carried out in order to give a better indication of how well the
hashing functions are performing. The reﬂection of the distribution of the W set can be
avoided by selecting a single ﬁxed w value to hash instead of using multiple values. The
same w value was therefore being evaluated 10.000 times. This revealed how randomly
the CRC hash function and the universal hash function would hash a ﬁxed value. The
histogram showing the hashing of the ﬁxed value w = 0x14EC can be seen in Figure 5.10.
The constant w = 0x14EC has simply been chosen randomly.
It is hard to draw any real conclusion from the results shown in Figure 5.9 and Figure
5.10. In order verify that the CRC hash function is satisfying has a χ2 goodness of ﬁt
test been carried out. This test will reject a null hypotheses if a p −value is too small.
The p-value is the evidence against the null hypothesis. A low p-value (p < 0.05) may
cast doubt on the validity of the null hypothesis [24]. The test will conﬁrm if the test
data ﬁts a normal distribution. Summation of several uniform distributed data sets will
result in a data set having a normal distribution. This is according to the central limit
theorem[35] and is sometime referred to as the uniform sum distribution. This analysis
is achieved by hashing multiple random W sets and summarize the many outputs. This
will be an investigation of the hashing method when a W set is randomly distributed. It
was previously mentioned that this would not give a true measure for the performance
since the distribution of the W set would be reﬂected in the results. The second test
would therefore sum hash values realized by multiple hashings of a randomly chosen ﬁxed
w value. In other words, each sum will be the result from a hashing of a W set containing
identical ﬁxed values. The Section 4.1 outlined that a good universal hash method would
output uniformly distributed hashes. This is why the distribution of the hash output is

5.5
Generating Test Data
The ACG methods are being tested with the universal hash function described in Section
4.1.1. The test is performed by the ACG Framework which is elaborated upon in Section
==PAGE:49==
5.6. ACG FRAMEWORK
39
5.6. The framework requires large sets of test data to determine the performance impact
when diﬀerent graph structures are being used. The CRC hash function evaluation also
requires large sets of test data.
Using a universal hash function will allow the performance analysis to be done without
any assumptions about the test data.
Alternatively one would have to assume some
kind of distribution of the test data in order to verify performance for best, average and
worst cases. It has therefore been chosen to evaluate graph structures with the use of a
universal hash function to avoid additional requirements to the test data. This allows easy
generation of the test data since it can be made without assumptions about the distribution
of the input keys. The CRC hash function method was also proved to perform well as
a universal hash function since the output was conﬁrmed to be well distributed. This
was shown in Section 5.4 in Table 5.1. The avoidance of assumptions about the test data
therefore still holds when the CRC hash function is used.
The test data only includes the generation of a W set with a given size. A C++ function
which would randomly permute a W set was therefore realized. The function can be found
in the source code in Appendix C.1. This made it very easy to randomly construct a W
sets consisting only of unique keys.
5.6
ACG Framework
An Acyclic Graph framework was initially developed in order to perform experimental
evaluations of diﬀerent graph structures which was detailed in Section 5.2.
The ﬁrst
developed program could generically be set to utilize any m-partite graph structure. The
UML diagram[13] and C++ source code for this program can be found in Appendix C.1.
The graph structures using a m-partite graph with m > 2 had to use two additional hash
functions to select between the subgraphs in which the Hleft and Hright would hash into.
An ad hoc technique partially inspired by the BDZ algorithm was developed in order to
solve this additional problem. This approach simply added two Hblockfrom and Hblockto
hash functions to the technique. These would select between subgraphs. The likely-hood
of selecting the same subgraph would be high.
This caused a high number of failing
attempts. The technique was therefore slightly modiﬁed such that if Hblockfrom = Bfrom
and Hblockto = Bto occurring would result in the lowest bit of Bfrom to be inverted since
Bfrom = Bto. This was considered to be fairly easy to implement in IC design. This
improved the technique but evaluation showed that it could not compete with the more
simple implementations of the complete- and bipartite graph structures. This initial ACG
framework would output a CSV ﬁles with the structure seen in Table 5.2.
The second ACG framework would evaluate the ﬁnal hashing technique which made use
of the hash family constructed by a programmable CRC polynomial. This design was
also used for the implementation of the ﬁnal C++ program. Therefore are the details
being outlined in Section 6.1. This ACG framework made it possible to compare the CRC
hashing approach against the universal hash function from Section 4.1. The output from
this evaluation would be a CSV ﬁle similar to the structure shown in Table 5.2.

5.7
The Algorithm
The ﬁnal algorithm has been developed based on the theory and evaluations described
in the previous sections. A comparison of the existing techniques indicated that the best
method was the CHM algorithm. The algorithm was already designed to construct order-
preserving hash functions. The CHM algorithm was categorized as an ACG method. That
conclusion led to the analysis of several graph structures which all could be used to con-
struct acyclic graphs. Theoretical results discussed in Section sec:analytical-investigation
suggested that the ACG method which utilized a complete graph structure would perform
better that any other structure. This was conﬁrmed by a practical evaluation that pro-
duced the results shown in Figure 5.8. The outcome from this can be seen in Section 5.4
in Figure 5.8. It was therefore concluded that an ACG method using a complete graph
structure should be used as the overlaying hash structure.
The other required feature was two universal hash functions.
These were developed
through the use of a CRC polynomial.
The CRC method was chosen since this kind
of hashing schemes had shown promising results in the paper discussed in Section 4.7.
A family of hash functions had to be constructed. The technique making this possible
was outlined in Section 5.3. It was discussed how a HCRC(w) function would be easier
to create if the requirements to the range of the hash output could be set from 0 to any
power of 2 number. This means a design utilizing a memory with 2x where x is any num-
ber, would be the easiest to create. The CRC hash function was benchmarked against a
well-known hash function from Section 4.1. The results was presented in Section 5.4 in
the Figure5.9 and Figure 5.10 and in Table 5.1. This veriﬁed that the CRC hash function
was outputting well distributed numbers. This concluded that the type of hash function
performed satisfactorily.
The developed algorithm can be seen in Equation 5.10 where A is the bit-width of the
address input port on the memory which is used to store the graph g in. The hash algo-
rithm is inspired by the CHM algorithm. The ﬁnal modulo-computation is deﬁned such
that it is ﬁxed to be done with a power of 2 number. This makes it possible to avoid a
divisor in the implemented design.
h(w) = (g((hCRC0(w)) + g (hCRC1(w))) mod 2A, where hCRC0, hCRC1 ∈{0, 1, ..., 2A −1}.
(5.10)
The algorithm meets the hashing requirements described in Section 3.1. The insertion of
==PAGE:51==
5.7. THE ALGORITHM
41
keys will however require a rehashing. This will update the HCRC0 and HCRC1 while the
graph assignment is also reprocessed. This means that the hardware structure will have
to be reprogrammed such that a new set of keys is supported. The same approach applies
when a key has to be deleted. This will, however, only mean that the hash for the deleted
key will be random instead of known. It might not be worth to consider deletion of keys
because of this. This concludes the development concerning the ﬁnal algorithm.
==PAGE:52==
==PAGE:53==
6
Implementation
The implementation includes two separate parts. The ﬁrst part is the implementation of a
software program which can compute and build the data ﬁles required by the second part.
The second part is the hardware component speciﬁed in Chapter 2. The software program
has been implemented in C++. This program will save three ﬁles. Two ﬁles used to setup
each of the CRC polynomials used by hCRC0 and hCRC1 from Equation (5.10). The third
ﬁle is written to a RAM which implements the g-function also from Equation (5.10). The
C++ program is a standalone part of the implementation. The rest of the documentation
of this is being detailed in Section 6.1. The rest of this Chapter will outline the hardware
implementation of the algorithm described in Section 5.7.
The hardware component has been implemented as a VHDL module and synthesized
by Intel’s Quartus Prime 16.1 program. The synthesis was made for an Altera Cyclone
IV. The ﬁnal synthesis report is presented in Section 8.4. A design overview can be seen
in Figure 6.1. This diagram simply sketches the complete design. The speciﬁcation from
Chapter 2 speciﬁed that the module had to perform key operations while concurrently
being able to be programmed by the CPU interface. This means that both the polyno-
mial data structure and the RAM structure had to be duplicated. The polynomial data is
stored in registers and referred to as PM0 data and PM1 data. Each of these is a pair of
generator matrices gP−1,47 used to realize hCRC0 and hCRC1. The CRC polynomials used
by these two hash functions were realized as a chain of XOR gates. In-between the XOR
gates would coeﬃcients be AND’ed together with an input key. The coeﬃcents are the
ones found in the generator matrix which was discussed in Section 5.3 and can be seen in
Equation (5.7). The graph g data is being stored in RAM0 and RAM1. The cfg_sel_i
signal dictates the conﬁguration being used. A conﬁguration uses either the PM00, PM01
and RAM0 set or the PM10, PM11 and RAM1 set. Only the conﬁguration not in use can be
accessed by the CPU interface.
The CPU has an interface to the pre_hash_RAM_ctrl module. This can be used to per-
form one of the following instructions: read/write to polynomials or read/write to RAMs.
Read and write instructions to the polynomials are performed in less clock cycles than
instructions to the RAM modules. The PM0 data and PM1 data registers can be seen
on Figure 6.1. The registers are simply a part of the prehashing_interface_setup
process. Graph data is passed to either RAM0 or RAM1. How to perform the diﬀerent
instructions is documented in Section 6.4. How the execution of the CPU operations are
being handled by the pipeline structure is detailed in Section 6.5.
Separate from the CPU interface is the key operation interface. This includes the following
signals: request signal, the input key signal and the conﬁguration signal. These are named
req_i, key_i and cfg_sel_i, respectively. These were described in the speciﬁcation
in Chapter 2.
The VHDL source code implementing the design can be found in Appendix D.1. Ver-
iﬁcation of the implementation is elaborated upon in Chapter 7. The ﬁnal VHDL imple-
mentations is made such that the generic constants CPU_AW and the IXW will setup
a solution which will use a matching number of memory addresses. This has made It
possible to quickly implement several versions of the cam_perf_hash component. In
43
==PAGE:54==
44
CHAPTER 6. IMPLEMENTATION
total has three versions been veriﬁed. These are using diﬀerent sizes of memory addresses.
The evaluated solutions are using the following number of memory addresses: 4k, 8k and
16k.

Figure 6.1: Implementation overview showing the top components and its subcomponents. The module
implements the algorithm described in Section 5.7. The CPU interface can program the PM0 and PM1
data sets. This will setup a new set of hash function: hCRC0 and hCRC1. The CPU interface will also be
used to setup the RAM0 and RAM1 components. This realizes the a g-function. A hash structure selected
by cfg_sel_i can then be used to perform key operations. First is hCRC0(key_i) and hCRC1(key_i)
performed by the prehashing_interface_setup. Each of the hash output is being used as address
in either RAM0 or RAM1. Thereby is an assigned vertex value being looked-up. These assigned values
are summarized and a modulo-operation is then performed. The modulo-computation is constructed such
that it is done with a power of 2 number. This means that a real modulo computation is in fact avoided.
Thereby is the ﬁnal hash presented in the ix_o output.
6.1
acghash_4kmem program
The hash structure depends on the ﬁles generated by a C++ program. The program
implements the algorithm deﬁned in Section 5.7.
The program requires the W set as
input.
This is given as a CSV ﬁle.
It further requires the user to set if the results
should be veriﬁed and to set the maximum allowed attempts. The ﬁnal arguments are
two ﬁlenames. The ﬁrst one will be used to name the data used for the PM data registers
and the second one is for the RAM data. A help text will be printed if one of these inputs
are not present. The help text is shown in Figure 6.2.

Figure 6.2: The acghash_4kmem program prints thisss help text if a wrong number of input arguments
are given.
The program reads the W set CSV ﬁle and start the construction of a graph by selecting
hCRC0 and hCRC1 randomly from the hash family. These two functions are used to hash
the W set and setup the edges in the graph. This is called the mapping step. Each edge
is thereby realized such that E = {{hCRC0(W), hCRC1(W)}}. This ends the mapping
step. The next step is the assignment step where the graph is traversed with a depth ﬁrst
search. This search assigns values to all the vertices such that Equation 5.10 can be used.
These values are the ones written to the RAM. The execution of the depth ﬁrst search will
simultaneously check the graph for cycles. If a cycle is encountered will two new hCRC0
and hCRC1 be selected and the process will restart the mapping step.
The mapping step has been improved such that self-loops and double edges are quickly
found. Self-loops are simply found by comparing hash values given by hCRC0 and hCRC1.
If both of these hash functions hashes to the same vertex is a self-loop detected. Double
edges is detected by hashing the edge set to edge_hash. This is implemented as a un-
ordered_map container [36]. The edge sets are sorted ﬁrst such that their direction does
not inﬂuence the hash. The unordered_map container makes it very fast to check if an
edge already exists. This makes it faster to determine if a new edge will results in double
edges. Early detection of self-loops and double edges improves the speed signiﬁcantly since
non valid solutions are discarded earlier in the process.
The class diagram is similar to the ACG framework used to evaluate the design which
was detailed in Section 5.6. The class diagram used to implement the graph structure can
be seen in Figure 6.3. The class diagram used by the hash structure is shown in Figure 6.4.
This speciﬁc program is used to construct the PM data and RAM data for a hardware
hash structure which utilizes a RAM with 4095 memory addresses. This means that each
of the hash functions hCRC0 and hCRC1 hashes into a graph with size 4095. This is realized
by constructing hCRC0 and hCRC1 such that each will output a 12 bit number. Realization
of the identity matrix has to match this criteria as discussed in Section 5.3. The identity
matrix has been constructed with a Matlab script found in Appendix A.1.7. This matrix
is deﬁned as a constant in the C++ program and used to ﬁrst build the F matrix from
Equation 5.9 by inserting a random generated polynomial and then computing F 48. The
F 48 matrix will be subject to a modulo-2 calculation such that the ﬁnal identity matrix
is realized. After this is the generator matrix with dimensions 12x48 being saved. A valid
==PAGE:56==
46
CHAPTER 6. IMPLEMENTATION
generator matrix can then be used to construct hCRC0 and hCRC1 in hardware.

6.2
acghash_8kmem program
An acghash_8kmem C++ program was also carried out to accommodate the need for a
hash which could support a larger W set. The UML class diagram is identical to the one
deﬁning the acghash_4kmem program. The only diﬀerence is the hCRC0 and hCRC1 now
hashes into a 13 bit number which makes addressing of 8192 addresses possible. The type
of the identity matrix F had to be changed into an unsigned long long from a size_t. This
was necessary since the computation of F 48 would result in numbers larger than what a
32 bit type can hold. The construction of the identity matrix was built by the Matlab
script found in Appendix A.1.7. The implementation can be found in Appendix C.4.
6.3
acghash_16kmem program
An acghash_16kmem C++ program was built in order to generate the PM data and the
RAM data used by a hash structure which utilizes a RAM with 16384 addresses. The
implementation of this is identical to the acghash_8kmem program.
This is with the
exception of the functions hCRC0 and hCRC1 which will now produce hashes as 14 bit
numbers. The implementation can be found in Appendix C.5.

6.4
Module Interface
The module interface was speciﬁed in Chapter 2. The CPU address signal is used to access
diﬀerent part of the hash structure.
The conﬁguration signal cfg_sel_i ultimately
dictates the hash structure in use and therefore also which part that is currently in idle.
The hash structure in idle i.e. the one not in use can be accessed by the CPU. The most
signiﬁcant bit of the CPU address input is used to select between access to PM data
registers or to the RAM modules. Selecting RAM access will simply allow the CPU to
write to the RAM module currently not in use. If a PM data access is requested will the
second most signiﬁcant bit of the CPU address be used to further specify which PM that
is accessed i.e. if it is data used by hCRC0 or hCRC1. The addressing is speciﬁed in Table
6.1. If the CPU interface tries to access the non-valid addresses will an acknowledgment
responds still be send on the cpu_rdy_o signal. This will not have any eﬀect on the
design. This simply secures that the CPU interface does not stall if a faulty command
was executed.

in an acknowledgment on the cpu_rdy_o signal which prevents the interface from hanging.
6.5
Pipeline Structure
The system has been implemented as a pipeline such that a throughput of one key per
clock cycle can be maintained. This was a requirement deﬁned in Chapter 3. The imple-
mentation uses at least 5 pipeline stages while key operations skips the last stage. The
last stage is only used by the CPU interface. The number of stages can be adjusted such
that more registers can be added to the outputs from the RAMs. That can be done to
accommodate potential needs for extra stages if the place and route process requires this.
The number of stages just after the RAMs is chosen by a RAM_REG_N constant in the
source ﬁle. The implemented design has been documented with two block diagrams shown
in Figure 6.5 and Figure 6.6. The complete design was divided into two ﬁgures just to
ﬁt the illustrations on the page. The insides of the acg_hash module includes multiple
pipeline registers. This part is therefore being illustrated separately in Figure 6.6. The
block diagram of the top module is the one shown in Figure 6.5. Both of the shown block
diagrams are modiﬁed such that the pipeline registers is illustrated. The number of stages
which the design uses can thereby easily be seen. It also gives a better understanding of
==PAGE:59==
6.5. PIPELINE STRUCTURE
49
the complete pipeline structure.
The top module connects the CPU interface and the key operation interface with the
prehash_RAM_ctrl module. The output from prehash_RAM_ctrl module is con-
nected to the next stage. A CPU read instruction request to the RAM data registers
will result in the instruction and write data being passed to the acg_hash module. The
prehash_RAM_ctrl module will handle the decoding of the CPU instruction. This de-
coding follows the protocol discussed in Section 6.4. Thereby is the RAM0 and RAM1
interface signals from the prehash_RAM_ctrl module being set up. This makes the
signals very easy to handle inside the acg_hash module. The RAM interface can in
fact be connected directly to the RAM modules inside the acg_hash module. The RAM
signals from the prehash_RAM_ctrl module will not be aﬀected if the CPU instruction
was an access to the PM registers. The access to PM registers is directly handled by the
prehash_RAM_ctrl module itself. It is important to notice that PM* data read data
is bypassing the acg_hash component. This makes reads of PM data registers faster
since less clock cycles are required. The discussed connections can be seen in Figure 6.5.
The prehash_RAM_ctrl component will either put CPU instructions on the RAM0
interface and then use the RAM1 interface for key operations or vice versa. The signal
RAM_cpu_rddata_sel_o is passed to the acg_hash module to indicate which RAM
interface that is being used by the CPU.

An example showing the execution of a CPU write-instruction together with a simulta-
neous key operation has been made. This illustrates how the signals in the top mod-
ule pipeline behave.
The timing diagram showing the example can be seen in Figure
6.7.
The inputs to the cam_perf_hash component is the CPU instruction together
with a key input. The signals involved in the key operation have been highlighted in
green.
It is seen that the input key simply ripples through the pipeline such that a
later comparison can be performed. The key is hashed by the HCRC0 and HCRC1 inside
the prehashing_RAM_ctrl component.
The outputs from this module are catego-
rized as prehashing signals.
The HCRC0 and HCRC1 functions outputs the hashes to
the ha_cpu_a_addr_RAM0_o and ha_b_addr_RAM0_o signals of the RAM0 interface.
The RAM0 interface is selected by the cfg_sel_i signal which dictates that RAM0 is
the active conﬁguration. The hashes computed by the prehashing step are used to lookup
corresponding values in the RAM0 module. These values will be added and then subject
to a modulo-computation. Recall that a real modulo-computation was avoided by the con-
struction of the developed algorithm. The ﬁnal value is presented on the hash_value_o
output. This is signaled ready by the cpu_rdy_0 signal. The CPU write instruction to
the RAM1 module is performed simultaneously. The CPU address signal is highlighted in
blue. The address signal is decoded by the prehashing_RAM_ctrl component which
uses the most signiﬁcant bits to select between PM register access and RAM access. The
==PAGE:61==
6.5. PIPELINE STRUCTURE
51
rest of the address signal is simply passed on through the RAM1 interface. This is passed
together with the CPU data that is being written. This data is highlighted in yellow.
Both the address data and the write data can be seen on the RAM1 interface from the
prehashing_RAM_ctrl component. The execution of the write instruction will take
some cycles to be performed by the acg_hash component. The instruction will be ac-
knowledged at last. The acknowledge signal is handled by the CPU_read_ctrl process
and ﬁnally outputted from the cam_perf_hash top module.

Figure 6.7: Timing diagram illustrating how key operations are performed while the CPU interface writes
data to the RAM module not in use by the key operation. The signals concerning the key operations have
been highlighted in green. The example show how the original key ripples through the pipeline. The pre-
hashing component outputs two hash values on the ha_cpu_a_addr_RAM0_o and ha_b_addr_RAM0_o
signals. These were generated through the use of HCRC0 and HCRC1. The hash values are being used to
lookup their corresponding values in the active RAM0 module which is why the values are present on the
RAM0 interface. The RAM0 module was selected as the active conﬁguration by the cfg_sel_i signal.
The values are being looked up internally in the acghash module. This will take some cycles to perform.
The ﬁnal results is being presented on the hash_value_o signal and validated by the hash_rdy_o signal.
The write instruction executed by the CPU interface is aﬀecting the RAM1 module since this structure is
currently idle. The CPU address data is highlighted in blue while the data being written is highlighted in
yellow. The data will simply be written to the RAM module and an acknowledgment signal will be passed.
This acknowledgement signal will ﬁnally be handled by the CPU_read_ctrl process and outputted on
the cpu_rdy_o signal.
==PAGE:63==
7
Veriﬁcation
The ﬁnal product has been thoroughly simulated and tested in order to veriﬁed that
the behavior of both the C++ programs and the ﬁnal cam_perf_hash component are
working as intended. The veriﬁcation concerning the C++ program is discussed in Section
7.1. The hardware part has been tested with the use of ModelSim. The program has been
used to simulate behavior of the design. This is being discussed in Section 7.2.
7.1
C++ Programs
Throughout the development has the C++ programs been veriﬁed by code inspection.
Every one of the ﬁnal programs can be set to verify the generated hash structure data.
This is done by evaluation of the input W with the use of the realized hash structure.
Each of the keys in the W set are being hashed and by comparing the hashes with the
order of the original key in the set W is the result being veriﬁed. The order of the key
in W must be equal to the hash value for that key. This is due to the fact that the hash
function is order-preserving.
The construction of the CRC polynomial hash family was done with help from a developed
Matlab script. This script generated a identity matrix which was deﬁned as a constant
in the C++ program and thereby used to deﬁne the CRC hash family.
The identity
matrix was veriﬁed with the help from two web services which provides VHDL code for
user-speciﬁed polynomials[12, 31]. The web tools made it possible to correlate Matlab
generated CRC expressions with the ones generated by the web services. This was used
to verify the realization of the CRC polynomials.
The ﬁnal C++ programs were used to generate data for the three versions of VHDL
implementations. This has shown to work as intended. It is therefore concluded that the
developed programs are working properly.
7.2
VHDL Implementation
The VHDL design has been simulated with ModelSim. The design can generically be
set to implement the several hash structures using diﬀerent sized memories. The veri-
ﬁcation process has been evaluating several design versions with 3 diﬀerent number of
memory addresses available. The evaluated versions are: 4k, 8k and 16k number memory
addresses. Each of these conﬁgurations has been veriﬁed which each of their own VHDL
testbench. The testbenches are very similar. Only conﬁguration of the constants CPU_WA
and the IXW together with the constant RAM_SIZE are diﬀerent in the three versions of
testbenches. Every design version was veriﬁed to perform one key operation per clock
cycle. Thereby is the throughput of one key operation per clock cycle being fulﬁlled.
The testbench used to simulate the design using 4k memory addresses deﬁnes the con-
stants as: RAM_SIZE = 4096, CPU_WA=14 and IXW=12. This testbench can be found
in Appendix D.2.1.
The design allowing 8k memory addresses to be used deﬁnes the
constants as: RAM_SIZE = 8192, CPU_WA=15 and IXW=13.
The testbench is shown
in Appendix D.2.2. Finally can the last testbench simulate a design with 16k memories
by setting the following constants: RAM_SIZE = 16384, CPU_WA=16 and IXW=14. The
53
==PAGE:64==
54
CHAPTER 7. VERIFICATION
constant named RAM_SIZE is only used internally by the testbenches. Another constant
named POLY_SIZE is also deﬁned in each testbench. This constant is also only used
internally in the testbenches. This POLY_SIZE dictates the width of the polynomial and
is therefore set to POLY_SIZE = 48 for all the designs. This remains ﬁxed for all the
designs due to the fact the width of the input key has been ﬁxed to 48 bits.
Each of the testbenches uses a state machine which is used to direct the commands which
programs the internal hash structures through the CPU interface. The state machine
deﬁnes 21 states which the testbench goes through. The sequence of these 21 states can
be seen in Figure 7.1. The ﬁrst part programs the hash structure used by conﬁguration 0.
The next step programs the hash structure used by conﬁguration 1. The following steps
simply verify that both hash structures can be read from.

The cam_perf_hash component is ﬁrst ready for key operations when conﬁguration 0

random times in order to verify that key operations can be done simultaneously with any
CPU interface operations.
The C++ programs: acghash_4kmem, acghash_8kmem and the acghash_16kmem
was used to generate RAM data and PM data for the three versions of the cam_perf_hash
components. Each of the versions has been simulated and their waveforms have been
inspected. This has concluded that the implemented design is working as intended.
==PAGE:65==
8
Results
Several evaluations have been carried out in order to document the performance of the
developed system. The developed technique is also being compared with a solution made
by Altera Denmark Technology Centre A/S. This comparison is presented in Section
8.1. The performance and scalability results can be seen in Section 8.2. Results from a
practical timing benchmarking are presented in Section 8.3. Finally is the results from
three synthesis outlined in Section 8.4.
8.1
Comparison with Altera hashing scheme
A hashing scheme developed by Altera Denmark Technology Centre A/S has been com-
pared against the developed acghash technique. The hashing scheme realized by Altera is
using two parametric hash functions which would hash 40 bit numbers into 10 bit num-
bers. The rest of the scheme is based on the CHM algorithm which is using a bipartite
graph. The two parametric hash functions were used together with the CHM algorithm
as a replacement for the universal hash functions. The size of the constructed bipartite
graph would be limited to a size of 2048 vertices (2k memory addresses). This is due to
the fact that the two parametric hash functions can only hash into 10 bit numbers i.e.
each of the two subparts in the graph hold: 210 = 1024 vertices.
A acghash_2kmem version was realized such that the technique developed through this
thesis could be compared with the hashing method developed by Altera. This version
would only support 40 bit keys and the design would use 2048 memory addresses.
Each of the programs was evaluated with several W sets with sizes ranging from 200 to
2000 in steps of 100. Each individual evaluation was limited such that a maximum of
40.000 attempts would limit each trial. The results from the evaluation can be seen in
Figure 8.1. It can be seen that the hashing scheme developed by Altera does not show
evaluations of W sets larger than 1300 keys.
The reason for this is that the hashing
scheme could not successfully ﬁnd valid solutions for W sets larger than 1300 within the
limitation of 40.000 attempts.
The performance of the parametric hash functions which is used to replace the universal
hash functions in the CHM algorithm has also been tested. This test is similar to the one
used to verify the performances of both the CRC hash function and the universal hash
function. The same test was used in Section 5.4 as a part of the experimental evaluation.
A W set including 4095 unique keys which were randomly distributed was used. Each of
these keys was hashed by the parametric hash function. The results from this can be seen
in Figure 8.2 (a). This result suggests at ﬁrst sight a good performance. The distribution
of the random W is, however, reﬂected in these results. Therefore was a random ﬁxed key
W = x14EC hashed 10.000 times. The result from this can be seen in Figure 8.2 (b). It
can be noticed that many hash values simply not occurs while a few hashes has a high
number of occurrences.
A χ2 goodness of ﬁt test similar to the one performed on the developed CRC hashing
schemes has also been used to verify the performance of the Altera hashing scheme. The
results are shown in Table 8.1. This shows that the Altera hashing function cannot reject
the null hypothesis at a 5% signiﬁcance level. The p-value is also seen to be very low.

Performance and Scalability

acghash_16kmem has been evaluated. The test was using random generated W sets with
sizes ranging from 3000 to 16000 in steps of 100. Each ﬁxed sized W set was evaluated
100 times. Each run required several attempts before a valid solution could be found.
The total amount of attempt required for the 100 evaluations was stored. The average
number of attempts was thereby easy to compute by dividing the total number of attempts
with 100. The result from the evaluation can be seen in Figure 8.3. The total number
of evaluated W sets is: 11, 51 and 131 for the by acghash_4kmem, acghash_8kmem
and acghash_16kmem, respectively. The maximum W sizes evaluated with each of the
programs has been indicated in Figure 8.3.

Synthesis Report
The implemented VHDL design was synthesized with the Quartus Prime 16.1 Lite Edition
provided by Intel[2].
The design was constrained to run with a 125 MHz clock.
The
constrain ﬁle can be found in Appendix D.3.1. Three versions of the implementation were
synthesized. These version was implementations which each utilized 4096 (4k), 8192 (8k)
and 16383 (16k) memory addresses.

synthesis showed that the implementation could run with a maximum frequency of 142.82

these are used.
The report summary from the synthesis which realized a 8k version can be seen in Table
8.5. This design uses 52 M10k memory blocks and the implementation can run with a
maximum frequency of 138.66 MHz.
The last synthesis report summary shows the results of the 16k version and can be seen in
Table 8.6. The design now uses 112 M10k memory blocks and can run with a maximum
frequency of 140.65 MHz.

9
Discussion and Conclusion
The solution developed in this thesis includes two main parts. One part is the software
used to construct data ﬁles which are used to program the second part. The second part
is a VHDL module which will perform the hashing of keys in a FPGA. This module can
be used as a replacement for a CAM module. The intension has been to allow a low-area
hardware solution. This has been a crucial consideration throughout the development of
the software program.
The speciﬁcation provided by Altera Denmark Technology Centre A/S has been used
to realize the ﬁnal VHDL component named cam_perf_hash. This speciﬁcation was
presented in Chapter 2. The ﬁnal component has fulﬁlled the speciﬁcation. Requirements
to the ﬁnal solution was outlined in Section 3. A performance requirement to the VHDL
component was that the design should be able to handle a throughput of one key operation
per clock cycle. The ﬁnal solution has been veriﬁed to fulﬁll this requirement. This was
veriﬁed by a simulation and discussed in Section 7.2. The requirements included three
algorithms. The ﬁrst one being essential since this dictated that a perfect hash function
should be realized. This has been achieved by a software program. The algorithm con-
structing such a hash function is built as a C++ program. This program is based on two
parts. The ﬁrst part is an overlaying layer providing and algorithm which allows order
preserving hash functions to be realized. This algorithm furthermore requires two univer-
sal hash functions to be available. The second part of the design includes the development
of a hardware implementation friendly version of a universal hash function. The ﬁrst part
was based on the CHM algorithm while the second part is based on the usage of CRC
polynomials which was used to construct a hash family of universal hash functions. The
choice of the CHM algorithm-inspired solution was due to a survey. The survey evaluated
several perfect hashing algorithms. These were all presented in Chapter 4. This survey
clariﬁed the many possibilities of realizing a perfect hash function. A categorization was
established in order to quickly determine the fundamentals of these algorithms. The in-
tension is that this categorization can be used to quickly identify key properties of hashing
algorithms. It was generally discovered that it was relatively hard to determine the pros
and cons between two algorithms. The categorization was able to help since the type of
an algorithm could quickly be determined. This made it clear that many algorithms were
based on graphs. This made it easier to conduct a parameter which could be used for
comparison. The many techniques was compared and evaluated in Chapter 5. A compar-
ison between the graph sizes which each of the algorithms would demand was carried out
and used to clarify that the best suitable solution was the CHM algorithm. This speciﬁc
approach was chosen since it showed to make use of a relatively small graph compared to
the other algorithms. This was in line with the non-functional requirements presented in
Section 3.2. The requirement stated that a low-area consumption was desirable compared
to processing time of Algorithm 1. The choice of the CHM algorithm which was a graph
based technique formed the basis for an investigation of graphs. Basic graph theory was
covered in order to investigate the graph structure that would help to build a hardware
component with low-area consumption. This theory concluded that the graph structure
allowing the highest number of keys to exist was a complete graph. This would aﬀectively
allow the smallest area consumption by a hardware implementation. This graph structure
63
==PAGE:74==
64
CHAPTER 9. DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSION
was contrary to the one used by the CHM algorithm. That technique is using a bipartite
graph in order to avoid self-loops. The ﬁrst part of the hash function is therefore only told
to be inspired by the CHM algorithm since the two approaches diﬀers in graph structures.
The impact of the potential generation of self-loops was even found to be relatively small.
This was due to the fact that the software implementation could quickly determine the
generation of a self-loop before the ﬁrst search was started. The depth ﬁrst search is the
most computation-heavy part of the software so avoiding this is a good improvement.
Thereby would an attempt resulting in a self-loop quickly be processed and discarded as
being a valid choice of graph.
The non-functional requirements outlined that a mathematical proof was necessary in or-
der to conﬁrm that a given technique would be able to support enough combinations. The
theorem related to spanning trees in graphs conﬁrmed this. The theorem was presented in
the graph theory evaluation. The CHM algorithm was describing a perfect hash function
hence the insertion and deletion of keys would not be possible. Algorithm 2 and 3 pre-
sented in the requirements demanded this feature. These can, however, still be emulated
by the use of Algorithm 1. This is simply done by adding a new key to the CSV ﬁle used
by the acghash programs which will generate the needed ﬁles used by a hash structure
such that the new set is supported. This will, however, require a complete new hash struc-
ture to be programmed. That will result in longer processing times of Algorithm 2. This
was, however, outlined in the non-functional requirements as a trade-oﬀbetween process
time of the required Algorithms and the hardware area consumption. It is believed that
the overhead in a hardware implementation which would natively support Algorithm 2
and 3 will be too high in regards to area consumption. For that reason were techniques
supporting these algorithms being discarded relatively early in the thesis.
The second part of the software implementation demanded two universal hash functions
to be available. This made it necessary to develop a method which allowed such function
to be realized in a FPGA. It was clear that a CRC polynomial would be easy to develop
in an FPGA. The hashing scheme making use of CRC polynomials was evaluated in a sur-
vey presented in Section 4.7. This survey found that the CRC polynomial would provide
an excellent hash function. This conclusion inspired an implementation of a hash family
based on programmable CRC polynomials. The developed technique showed satisfying
results when experimental evaluation was carried out. The evaluation was presented in
Section 5.4. There might be a better test for determination of a hash functions perfor-
mance. It might be worth to consider the same approach used by Jain, R[21] were the
entropy of several hash functions are being used as basis for comparison of several hashing
schemes. The ﬁnal algorithm was detailed in Section 5.7. This would make use of the hash
family of CRC polynomials as the underlying layer used to realize a complete graph. The
overlaying layer was the graph structure which essentially realizes the order-preserving
hash function. The advantage of this ﬁnal algorithm is that it is a very simple structure.
It is even simpler than the one developed by Altera Denmark Technology Centre A/S.
The ﬁnal hardware implementation beneﬁt from this simplicity since the implementation
becomes small. The ﬁnal algorithm was using a modulo-operator. This is not desired in
hardware. The modulo-operation was therefore ﬁxed such that only computation of power
of 2 numbers was considered. This can be done without a modulo-operation.
The developed algorithm is relatively easy to implement as a VHDL module. The VHDL
module is easy to program through the CPU interface. This makes it possible to program
the hash structures which include the RAM module and the PM0 data and PM1 data
==PAGE:75==
65
registers. The HCRC0 and HCRC1 functions was thereby realized in the VHDL module.
This technique is considered a very good approach. The implementation of CRC polynomi-
als are widely used in FPGA designs. Many articles further describes the implementation
of programmable CRC modules [37, 38, 22]. The output from the two hash functions was
used to lookup assignment values in a graph. The graph was realized as one RAM module.
RAM blocks in a FPGA are considered a cheap resource for storage of large content. The
design can easily be updated to match any physical RAM resource available in a given
FPGA. This means that a design can essentially make full use of the speciﬁc RAM blocks
available in a FPGA. It will be more reasonable to let the physical RAM storage available
dictate a design solution even if a small W set is required. That would relax the process
of running Algorithm 1 and make full use of a RAM resource. The two hash functions will
make hash values used as address inputs to the RAM module. These will therefore have
to match the address space of the RAM. The extra address space will require a relatively
low increase in the area used to implement the two HCRC0 and HCRC1 function. This is,
however, considered a small price compared to the loss of wasting useful RAM resources.
The content of the memory slot would be the assigned values. These assigned values are
programmed into the RAM module through the CPU interface. The content is computed
with the use of the software. The looked up values are simply added and the lowest bits
are extracted in order to perform the modulo-like operation. The ﬁnal VHDL component
showed to fulﬁll all the speciﬁcations together with the given requirements. The perfor-
mance of Algorithm 2 and 3 will, however, have to be done through the use of Algorithm 1.
The performance of the implemented software has been tested.
The results was pre-
sented in Chapter 8. The ﬁrst test was a comparison of the developed technique against
a solution developed by Altera. The results was shown in Figure 8.1. The developed
technique was adapted to match the speciﬁcations of the Altera technique. These results
showed that the algorithm could build a hash structure supporting 2000 keys within the
limit of 40.000 attempts. This is a 97% utilization of the total memory addresses available.
The technique used by Altera could support a set of 1300 keys with the same number of
memory addresses available within the same limit of 40.000 attempts. This is only a 63%
utilization of the memory addresses. Solutions supporting 1300 keys would use 64.52 at-
tempts in average in order to ﬁnd a valid hash structure. The developed technique was
able to support 1800 keys with 40.57 attempts in average. This is a 38% memory saving
compared to the technique developed by Altera. The results from the evaluation even
show that 2000 keys can be hashed within a reasonable time frame. This is an even higher
memory saving of 53%. The lowered memory usage is due to the choice of a complete
graph versus a bipartite graph which is used by Altera. The developed technique would
theoretically be able to support keys sets with up to 2047 keys since 2048 memory ad-
dresses will be available to the construction of a graph. This was deﬁned by Equation
eq:ACG-lower-bound. This limit will, however, take very long time to reach since many
attempts will have to be expected. The same limit will be theoretically true for the tech-
nique used by Altera. This will be much harder to reach since a fraction of the spanning
trees in the graph will be accessible since the graph is bipartite.
Altera is making use of parametric hash functions which are covering the same operations
as the CRC hash functions used in the developed design.
The parametric hash func-
tion was evaluated with the similar approach used to test the CRC hash functions. This
test showed that if a ﬁxed value was hashed many times would the output not be well
distributed. This was conﬁrmed by a χ2 goodness of ﬁt test. This would aﬀect the per-
==PAGE:76==
66
CHAPTER 9. DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSION
formance of the technique developed by Altera since the solution relies on an assumption
of well distributed keys. Such a technique would have to consider worst, best and average
cases.
The performance together with an evaluation of the scalability for three versions of the
design was tested. The results from the test of the three programs: acghash_4kmem,
acghash_8kmem and acghash_16kmem can be seen in Section 8.2 on Figure 8.3. Sev-
eral sizes of a W set have been tested. The W sizes larger than |W| = 4000 has shown to
drastically increase the number of required attempts when a solution has to be found by
the acghash_4kmem program. It is, however, expected that key sets with just a few keys
more than 4000 will still be manageable to support. This is simply a trade-oﬀbetween
area and the process time involved in the execution of the acghash_4kmem program.
This tendency is the same for all the acghash versions. It can generally be said that a W
set with a size larger than 80% of the available memory addresses will involve an extra
ordinary number of attempts. The presented results also shows software version using a
bipartite instead of a complete graph. This conﬁrms the theory outlining that a complete
graph structure would allow the most spanning trees hence increase performance. The
diﬀerence between the complete graph and the bipartite graph structure is quite drastic.
The acghash programs using a complete graph generally performs 24% better than the
bipartite versions. This means that 24% more keys can be supported by the acghash
program which uses a complete graph.
The scalability of the acghash programs also shows to be better than the bipartite ver-
sions. This is partially due to the fact that a bipartite structure will prevent more and
more potential edge conﬁgurations compared to a complete graph structure. The theory
outlining this was elaborated upon in Section 5.2. It could be seen in Figure 5.3 that the
ratio between edge conﬁgurations between a complete and bipartite graph would increase
as the size of the graph became bigger. This eﬀectively means that the performance dif-
ference between the two will be larger for designs utilizing large memory blocks.
A
practical
test
of
the
run
times
for
the
three
programs:
acghash_4kmem,
acghash_8kmem and acghash_16kmem was carried out and presented in Section 8.3.
The acghash_4kmem program was compiled with both the Intel C++ compiler and the
Microsoft Visual C++ compiler. The evaluation showed that there was a relatively large
diﬀerence between the compilers. The Intel C++ compiler builds a version of the program
that is roughly 30% faster.
A second practical test was carried out in order to give an impression of the run times
for evaluation of diﬀerent key sets with the three acghash_4kmem, acghash_8kmem
and acghash_16kmem programs. The size of a key set that can be supported with an
average run time lower than 1 second has been indicated in Figure 8.5. These results are
meant as a help when one has to make a design choice. The results are correlated with the
ones shown in Section 8.2 which presented the average number of attempts required for
diﬀerent key sets. It is interesting to compare these two results. It can be seen that the
run times for the acghash_8kmem and acghash_16kmem are following each other until
acghash_8kmem hits a turning point. The same behavior can be identiﬁed on Figure 8.3
where the average number of attempts are shown. This shows that the two programs also
follow each other but the acghash_8kmem are performing more attempts. This means
that more attempts are being evaluated compared to the acghash_16kmem but their
run times still shows the same evaluation time.
==PAGE:77==
9.1. OUTLOOK
67
Three versions of the cam_perf_hash components were synthesized, each would make
use of the ﬁles generated by the acghash_4kmem, acghash_8kmem and acghash_16kmem
programs. The VHDL modules diﬀerentiated only in the number of memory addresses
available to the hash structure. This would of course result in diﬀerent usage of mem-
ory blocks.
The results from the three synthesis was presented in Section 8.4.
The
cam_perf_hash providing 4k memory addresses had a usage of 24 memory blocks which
indicates that the two 4k addresses memory blocks RAM0 and RAM1 with a data width
of 12 bits each are being realized by cascading 6 M10k blocks. Each of the blocks thereby
provides 2 data bits. This matches the expectations to the ﬁnal hash function since it will
make hashes in the range 0 to 4095 which can be covered by 12 bits i.e. 2 × 6 = 12. The
addressing of them can be done without any address decoding since the address space can
be covered by one block. The total memory usage thereby becomes 24 M10k blocks since
one dual-port RAM will use 12 and two of these add up to 24 blocks.
The same calculation can be done for the design providing 8k memory addresses. Each
slot will now have to use 13 bit since the ﬁnal hash must realize hashes in the range 0
to 8192. This synthesis report indicates that the design is realized by cascading 13 M10k
blocks each providing 1 data bit. This add up to 13 × 2 × 2 = 52. This is also conﬁrmed
by the synthesis report seen in Table 8.5 which shows the same number of RAM blocks.
The ﬁnal design is using 112 RAM blocks. The address space can no longer be covered
by a single M10k block. This design will therefore include some logic controlling the ad-
dressing of the constructed memory block.
All the VHDL modules have been constrained to run with an operation speed of 125
MHz. Every design showed that it was possible for the synthesis tool to close timing. The
synthesis was for a Cyclone V which is a rather slow and small FPGA compared to the
fastest and largest once delivered by Altera. It is therefore believed that the design will
be able to run at a much faster speed.
9.1
Outlook
The solution has shown to be very ﬂexible in terms of memory usage. The bit width of
the input keys has not been discussed since it was a requirement that it should be ﬁxed
to 48 bits for the developed component. The design is, however, easy to adjust to another
key width. This can simply be done by adding or removing the number of coeﬃcients
used by the implemented CRC hash functions. This was actually done in order to adapt
the design to match the speciﬁcations of the hashing scheme developed by Altera. Both
the hardware and software parts will have to be adjusted manually.
The categorization of algorithms might be useful in the future when more algorithms
will have to be investigated. The categorization was heavily based on the technique found
in [15]. It has shown to help by quantifying the key aspects of the many hashing algo-
rithms. The work in this thesis has mostly been investigating graph based algorithms. It
is believed that order-preserving hashing schemes which falls into the category of graph
based approaches will allow the lowest area consumption. If a future hashing schemes is
not required to be order-preserving will other approaches most likely be preferable.
It might be worth to consider the usage of the FCD algorithm if new requirements al-
==PAGE:78==
68
CHAPTER 9. DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSION
low a throughput lower than one key operation per clock cycle.
The approach could
realize order-preserving hash functions while making use of a cyclic graph. The approach
was therefore categorized as a CG method. This would allow a more compact design hence
a hardware implementation would require less area. The method did, however, make use
of two consecutive lookups in the same memory space. This will limit the throughput and
was evaluated in Section 5.1.
9.2
Final Conclusion
The developed solution has shown to meet all the requirements. The VHDL module has
shown to be able to perform one key operation per cycle while still allowing another con-
ﬁguration to be programmed through a CPU interface. The developed cam_perf_hash
component meets the speciﬁcation given by Altera Denmark Technology Centre A/S. The
developed technique further proved to outperform an older technique developed by Altera.
The hash structure of the cam_perf_hash component will have to be programmed with
the use of three ﬁles. One ﬁle contains data for a RAM module. The other two will be
data used to program two hash functions.
The ﬁnal design will be useful in systems where Content Addressable Memory (CAM)
modules are required. The system can be used in many applications including the real-
ization of a packet parsing unit.

A.1

A.1.1
PHF_evaluations.m
1
% ------------------------------------------------------------------------
2
% Evaluation of several PHF function versus the orderpreserving approach of
3
% constuctring a acyclic graph. The area estimation are based on the work
4
% from the articles below:
5
%
6
% CMH based on:
7
% Czech, Z. J., Havas, G., & Majewski, B. S. (1992).
8
% An optimal algorithm for generating minimal perfect hash functions.
9
% Information Processing Letters, 43(5), 257-264.
10
% (Area estimation not based on this article)
11
%
12
% FCD based on:
13
% Fox, E. A., Chen, Q. F., Daoud, A. M., & Heath, L. S. (1990).
14
% Order preserving minimal perfect hash functions and information retrieval.
15
% http://doi.org/10.1145/96749.98233
16
%
17
% BMZ based on:
18
% C. Botelho, F., Kohayakawa, Y., & Ziviani, N. (2005).
19
% A practical minimal perfect hashing method. article.
20
% http://doi.org/10.1.1.91.6283
21
%
22
% BDZ based on:
23
% C. Botelho, F., Pagh, R., & Ziviani, N. (2012).
24
% Simple and space-efficient minimal perfect hash functions. article.
25
% http://doi.org/10.1.1.221.5411
26
%
27
% Author: Casper Munch Hansen
28
% ------------------------------------------------------------------------
29
30
close(allchild(0))
31
clc
32
format compact
33
34
% Size of the CMH acyclic graph approach according is according to the
35
% following article 2.09n vertices:
36
% C. Botelho, F., Kohayakawa, Y., & Ziviani, N. (2005).
37
% A practical minimal perfect hashing method. http://doi.org/10.1.1.91.6283
38
% The size can thereby be determined as(this one is order-preserving):
39
CMHsize = @(n) 2.09*n;
40
41
% FCD has tried their design with a ratio as low as 1.25. The algorithm is
42
% order preserving in itself. They says that the graph will have the size
43
% 1.25*edges where each edge is a key hence the size can be calculated as:
44
FCDsize =@(n) 1.25*n;
45
46
47
% BMZ argue that they can construct a cyclic graph with 1.15n vertices
48
% where n=|S|. This algorithm is not orderpresering meaning that the size
49
% of a RAM handling permutation after hashing must be added. The final size
50
% can therefor be described with the following equation:
51
BMZsize =@(n) 1.15*n+n;
% add n for permutation in RAM
52
53
% BDZ defines a lower bound for the a c constrant in size = c*n. This lower
54
% bound is c~=1.23. The algorithm is not orderpreserving hence the
55
% permuation RAM is added to the final equation:
56
BDZsize = @(n) 1.23*n+n; % add n for permutation in RAM
57
58
n=1:4095;
59
60
figure(1)
61
hold on;
62
hl(1) = plot(CMHsize(n),n);
63
hl(2) = plot(FCDsize(n),n);
64
hl(3) = plot(BMZsize(n),n);
65
hl(4) = plot(BDZsize(n),n);
66
5

void graph::clear_subblocks()
28
{
29
for (std::vector<vertices_block>::iterator it = subblocks.begin(); it != subblocks.end(); ++it) {
30
vertex_map_type *vmap = &it->vertex_map;
31

32
for (vertex_map_type::iterator itv = vmap->begin(); itv != vmap->end(); ++itv) {
33

vertices can be recognized)
34

// Remove edge addresses
35
itv->vstate = white;
// Reset marking to white
36
}

void graph::addedge(size_t blockID_from, size_t blockID_to, size_t from_ID, size_t to_ID, size_t
edge_label)
45
{
46
size_t sortedinput[4]; // sort inputs
47
//cout << "blockID_from: " << blockID_from << " blockID_to" << blockID_to << "vertexID_from " <<
from_ID << " vertexID_to" << to_ID << endl;
48
// Illigal edge detection:
49
if (subblocks.size() == 1) // Single block special case:
50
{
51
// Self-loop (only if single block exists)
52
if (to_ID == from_ID) {
53
cyclicFlag = true; // Self-loop created
54
//cout << "Self-loop error" << endl;
55
return;
56
}
57
sortBlocksVertices(sortedinput, blockID_from, blockID_to, from_ID, to_ID);
58
// Check if edge exists:
59
if (!add_key(0,0, (uint16_t)sortedinput[2], (uint16_t)sortedinput[3])) // If vertex pair is not
added it must exist
60
{
61
cyclicFlag = true; // Mulitple edges between vertices created
62
//cout << "Mulitple edges between vertices created error" << endl;
63
return;
64
}
65
// Add into edges vector:
66
edges.push_back(edge(sortedinput[0], sortedinput[1], sortedinput[2], sortedinput[3], edge_label,
white));
67
68
// Add edge address (edges.size()-1) to from and to vectors:
69
subblocks.at(blockID_from).vertex_map.at(from_ID).edges_adr.push_back((size_t)edges.size() - 1);
70
subblocks.at(blockID_to).vertex_map.at(to_ID).edges_adr.push_back((size_t)edges.size() - 1);
71
return;
72
}
73
else if(subblocks.size() == 2) { // Two blocks only (Self-loop and block cycle cannot exists)
74
// Check if edge exists:
75
sortBlocksVertices(sortedinput, blockID_from, blockID_to, from_ID, to_ID);
76
if (!add_key(0,1,(uint16_t)sortedinput[2], (uint16_t)sortedinput[3])) // If vertex pair is not
added it must exist
77
{
78
cyclicFlag = true; // Mulitple edges between vertices created
79
//cout << "Mulitple edges between vertices created error" << endl;
80
return;
81
}
82
}
else
// Multiple block cases:
83
{
84
// Block loop (only if more that 1 blocks exists):
85
if (blockID_from == blockID_to) {
86
cyclicFlag = true; // Block-loop created
87
//cout << "Block-loop created" << endl;
88
return;
89
}
90
// Check if edge exists:
91
sortBlocksVertices(sortedinput, blockID_from, blockID_to, from_ID, to_ID);
92
if (!add_key((uint16_t)sortedinput[0], (uint16_t)sortedinput[1], (uint16_t)sortedinput[2], (
uint16_t)sortedinput[3])) // If block pair and vertex pair can be added if not it must exist
93
{
94
cyclicFlag = true; // Mulitple edges between vertices created
95
//cout << "Mulitple edges between vertices created error" << endl;
96
return;
97
}
98
}
99
100
// Only goes here when more than one block is used:
101
// Add into edges vector:
102
edges.push_back(edge(blockID_from, blockID_to, from_ID, to_ID, edge_label, white));
103
104
// Add edge address (edges.size()-1) to from and to vectors:
105
subblocks.at(blockID_from).vertex_map.at(from_ID).edges_adr.push_back((size_t)edges.size() - 1);
106
subblocks.at(blockID_to).vertex_map.at(to_ID).edges_adr.push_back((size_t)edges.size() - 1);
107
}

void graph::sortBlocksVertices(size_t *sortedinput, size_t blockID_from, size_t blockID_to, size_t

114

116

}

121

123
}
124
125
// Sort vertices:
126
if (v_from < v_to) {
127
sortedinput[2] = v_from;
128
sortedinput[3] = v_to;
129
}
130
else {
131
sortedinput[2] = v_to;
132
sortedinput[3] = v_from;
133
}

void graph::DFSvisit(size_t elabel)
166
{
167
// Make aliases (with references):
168
vertex *v_from = &subblocks.at(edges.at(elabel).location.blockID_from_to.first).vertex_map.at(edges
.at(elabel).location.from_to.first);
169
vertex *v_to = &subblocks.at(edges.at(elabel).location.blockID_from_to.second).vertex_map.at(edges.
at(elabel).location.from_to.second);
170
171
searchCnt += 1;
// Track number of visited edges
172
edges.at(elabel).estate = black; // Mark edge as used
173
174
// Find edges to follow:
175
vector<size_t> e_next_adr;
176
if (v_from->vstate == white) {
177
v_from->vstate = gray;
// Set vertex as "inprogress"
178
e_next_adr = v_from->edges_adr; // Copy next edge addresses
179
v_from->assign_val = (edges.at(elabel).label - v_to->assign_val + n_edges) % n_edges; // Assign
value
180
//cout << "b[" << edges.at(elabel).location.blockID_from_to.first << "]v[" << v_from->adr << "]
-- ";
181
}
182
else if (v_to->vstate == white) {
183
v_to->vstate = gray;
// Set vertex as "inprogress"
184
e_next_adr = v_to->edges_adr;
// Copy next edge addresses
185
v_to->assign_val = (edges.at(elabel).label - v_from->assign_val + n_edges) % n_edges; // Assign
value:
==PAGE:114==
30
APPENDIX C. APPENDIX C - C++ PROGRAM
186
//cout << "b[" << edges.at(elabel).location.blockID_from_to.second << "]v[" << v_from->adr << "]
-- ";
187
}
188
else {
189
cyclicFlag = true;
190
return;
191
}
192
193
// Traverse all non-visited edges:
194
for (size_t i = 0; i < e_next_adr.size(); i++)
195
{
196
if (edges.at(e_next_adr.at(i)).estate == white) {
197
DFSvisit(edges.at(e_next_adr.at(i)).label);
198
if (cyclicFlag) {
199
return;
200
}
201
}
202
}
203
// No cycle found
204
}
205
206
void graph::DFStraverse()
207
{
208
size_t n_edges = edges.size();
209
searchCnt = 0;
210
211
// Visit edges in graph:
212
for (std::vector<edge>::iterator e = edges.begin(); e != edges.end(); ++e) {
213
// Follow non-visisted edges
214
// searchCnt equals number of visits i.e if this equals total number of edges then all have been
visited
215
if (e->estate == white && searchCnt < n_edges)
216
{
217
vertex *v = &subblocks.at(e->location.blockID_from_to.first).vertex_map.at(e->location.from_to.
first);
218
v->vstate = gray;
// Mark initial vertex as "inprogress"
219
v->assign_val = 0;
// Assign initial value
220
vector<size_t> e_next_adr = v->edges_adr; // Copy all edges addresses belonging to start vertex
221
222
// Traverse all edges connected to starting vertex:
223
for (size_t i = 0; i < e_next_adr.size(); i++)
224
{
225
//cout << "Start: b[" << e->location.blockID_from_to.first << "]v[" << v->adr << "] -- ";
226
if (edges.at(e_next_adr.at(i)).estate == white) {
227
DFSvisit(edges.at(e_next_adr.at(i)).label);
228
if (cyclicFlag) {
229
//cout << "DFS cycle found error" << endl;
230
return;
231
}
232
}
233
}
234
}
235
}
236
}

size_t hash_data::lookup(size_t key)
37
{
38
// Locate vertex memory address
39

53

break;

58

h_r_value = mem_blocks.at(subblock_adr_b).at(adr_mem_b);

break;
61
}

//cout << "H_l = " << h_l_value << " H_r = " << h_r_value << endl;
63

// Calculate final hash value and return:
65
return (h_l_value + h_r_value) % W_size;

// Hash function:

// General hash function placeholder used to quickly change hash function

size_t unihash(hpm h, size_t key)
172
{
173

176
size_t block_unihash(hpm h, size_t key, size_t n_blocks, bool blockFrom) {
177
size_t hash_value = unihash(h, key);
178
179
size_t KeySize = (size_t)log2(n_blocks);
180
181
size_t MSkey = hash_value >> (KeySize);
// Shift LSb out to obtain MSkey
182
size_t LSkey = hash_value & (n_blocks - 1);
// Remove x MSb to obtain LSkey
183
184
bitset<8> hash_value8b(hash_value);
185
bitset<8> MSkey8b(MSkey);
186
bitset<8> LSkey8b(LSkey);
187
188
//cout << "KeySize: " << KeySize << " n_blocks: " << n_blocks << endl;
189
//cout << "hash_value: " << hash_value8b << endl;
190
//cout << "MSkey:
" << MSkey8b << endl;
191
//cout << "LSkey:
" << LSkey8b << endl;
192
193
// Check if keys are identical and fix if nescessary
194
if (LSkey == MSkey) {
195
// Flip LSb (invert):
196
if (MSkey & 1) { // LSb must be 1
197
MSkey -= 1;
198
}
199
else {
200
MSkey += 1;
201
}
202
}
203
204
if (blockFrom) {
// Return "to" block(LSb)
205
return LSkey;
206
}
207
else {
// Return "from" block(MSb)
208
return MSkey;
209
}
210
}

/*
213
The following hash function is found in:
214
Cormen, T. H. et al. (2009). Introduction to algorithms (3rd ed.). book,
215
MIT Press. section 11.3 p. 267
216
*/
217
218
size_t unihash_cormen113(hpm h, size_t key)
219
{
220
return ((h.a*key + h.b) % h.p) % h.m;
221
}

233

}

void graph::DFSvisit(size_t elabel)
113
{
114
// Make aliases (with references):
115
vertex *v_from = &vertices.at(edges.at(elabel).FromTo.first);
116
vertex *v_to = &vertices.at(edges.at(elabel).FromTo.second);
117
118
searchCnt += 1;
// Track number of visited edges
119
edges.at(elabel).estate = black; // Mark edge as used
120
121
// Find edges to follow:
122
vector<size_t> e_next_adr;
123
if (v_from->vstate == white) {
124
v_from->vstate = gray;
// Set vertex as "inprogress"
125
e_next_adr = v_from->edges_adr; // Copy next edge addresses
126
v_from->assign_val = (edges.at(elabel).label - v_to->assign_val + assign_max) % assign_max; //
Assign value
127
//cout << "b[" << edges.at(elabel).location.blockID_from_to.first << "]v[" << v_from->adr << "]
-- ";
128
}
129
else if (v_to->vstate == white) {
130
v_to->vstate = gray;
// Set vertex as "inprogress"
131
e_next_adr = v_to->edges_adr;
// Copy next edge addresses
132
v_to->assign_val = (edges.at(elabel).label - v_from->assign_val + assign_max) % assign_max; //
Assign value:
133
//cout << "b[" << edges.at(elabel).location.blockID_from_to.second << "]v[" << v_from->adr << "]
-- ";
134
}
135
else {
136
cyclicFlag = true;
137
return;
138
}
139
140
// Traverse all non-visited edges:
141
for (size_t i = 0; i < e_next_adr.size(); i++)
142
{
143
if (edges.at(e_next_adr.at(i)).estate == white) {
144
DFSvisit(edges.at(e_next_adr.at(i)).label);
145
if (cyclicFlag) {
146
return;
147
}
148
}
149
}
150
// No cycle found
151
}

void graph::DFStraverse()

// Assign initial value
167
vector<size_t> e_next_adr = v->edges_adr; // Copy all edges addresses belonging to start vertex
168
169
// Traverse all edges connected to starting vertex:
170
for (size_t i = 0; i < e_next_adr.size(); i++)
171
{
172
//cout << "Start: b[" << e->location.blockID_from_to.first << "]v[" << v->adr << "] -- ";
173
if (edges.at(e_next_adr.at(i)).estate == white) {
174
DFSvisit(edges.at(e_next_adr.at(i)).label);
175
if (cyclicFlag) {
176
//cout << "DFS cycle found error" << endl;
177
return;
178
}
179
}
180
}
181
}
182
}
183
}

g_matrix hash_data::init_poly_matrix(const size_t poly_degree)

20
size_t hash_data::lookup(unsigned long long key)
21
{
22
// Locate vertex memory address
23
size_t adr_mem_a = unihash_crc(1, key);
24
size_t adr_mem_b = unihash_crc(0, key);
25
26
// Perform lookup (in one block only):
27
size_t h_a_value = mem_block.at(adr_mem_a);
28
size_t h_b_value = mem_block.at(adr_mem_b);
29
30
//cout << "H_l = " << h_l_value << " H_r = " << h_r_value << endl;
31
// Calculate final hash value and return:
32
return (h_a_value + h_b_value) % modulo_fix;
33
}

void hash_data::add_mem_info()
36
{
37
size_t a_unused_adr = 0;
38
for (size_t i = 0; i < mem_block.size(); i++) // all memories has equal size
39
{
40
if (mem_block.at(i) == W_size) {
41
a_unused_adr += 1;
42
}
43
}
44
mem_unused_cnt = a_unused_adr;
45
}

size_t hash_data::unihash_crc(bool select_poly_a, unsigned long long key)

{

53

58
{

60

[10] ^ ct[11] ^ ct[12] ^ ct[13] ^ ct[14] ^ ct[15] ^ ct[16] ^ ct[17] ^ ct[18] ^ ct[19] ^ ct

//cout << "b[" << edges.at(elabel).location.blockID_from_to.second << "]v[" << v_from->adr << "]
-- ";

}

}

}

}

// No cycle found

/* Hash function:
48
The following hash function is utilizing a CRC polynomial
49
*/
50
size_t hash_data::unihash_crc(bool select_poly_a, unsigned long long key)
51
{
52
const int poly_length = 13; // Remember to match memory addresses available
53
bitset<poly_length> hv;
54
bitset<48> d(key);
55
56
if (select_poly_a == 1) {
57
for (size_t i = 0; i < poly_length; i++)
58
{
59
bitset<48> ct = poly_matrix_a.at(i) & d;
60
hv.set(i, ct[0] ^ ct[1] ^ ct[2] ^ ct[3] ^ ct[4] ^ ct[5] ^ ct[6] ^ ct[7] ^ ct[8] ^ ct[9] ^ ct
[10] ^ ct[11] ^ ct[12] ^ ct[13] ^ ct[14] ^ ct[15] ^ ct[16] ^ ct[17] ^ ct[18] ^ ct[19] ^ ct
[20] ^ ct[21] ^ ct[22] ^ ct[23] ^ ct[24] ^ ct[25] ^ ct[26] ^ ct[27] ^ ct[28] ^ ct[29] ^ ct
[30] ^ ct[31] ^ ct[32] ^ ct[33] ^ ct[34] ^ ct[35] ^ ct[36] ^ ct[37] ^ ct[38] ^ ct[39] ^ ct
[40] ^ ct[41] ^ ct[42] ^ ct[43] ^ ct[44] ^ ct[45] ^ ct[46] ^ ct[47]);
61
}
62
}else {
63
for (size_t i = 0; i < poly_length; i++)
64
{
65
bitset<48> ct = poly_matrix_b.at(i) & d;
66
hv.set(i, ct[0] ^ ct[1] ^ ct[2] ^ ct[3] ^ ct[4] ^ ct[5] ^ ct[6] ^ ct[7] ^ ct[8] ^ ct[9] ^ ct
[10] ^ ct[11] ^ ct[12] ^ ct[13] ^ ct[14] ^ ct[15] ^ ct[16] ^ ct[17] ^ ct[18] ^ ct[19] ^ ct
[20] ^ ct[21] ^ ct[22] ^ ct[23] ^ ct[24] ^ ct[25] ^ ct[26] ^ ct[27] ^ ct[28] ^ ct[29] ^ ct
[30] ^ ct[31] ^ ct[32] ^ ct[33] ^ ct[34] ^ ct[35] ^ ct[36] ^ ct[37] ^ ct[38] ^ ct[39] ^ ct
[40] ^ ct[41] ^ ct[42] ^ ct[43] ^ ct[44] ^ ct[45] ^ ct[46] ^ ct[47]);
67
}
68
}
69
return (size_t)hv.to_ulong();
70
}

// Verification:

bool hash_data::verify_hash(vector<unsigned long long> *W)

{

bool acg_complete(hash_data *C, // hash data to manipulate

50

52

==PAGE:177==
D
Appendix D - FPGA
Implementation
D.1
VHDL Implementation
D.1.1
cam_perf_hash.vhd
1
-- ##########################################################################
2
--
3
-- Module:
cam_perf_hash
4
--
5
-- Description: Core that implements a CAM function using a perfect hash
6
--
algorithm.
7
--
8
--
Background:
9
--
10
--
In network packet processing, numerous lookups are
11
--
needed for MAC addresses, VLAN or MPLS labels, IP addresses
12
--
etc.
13
--
It is generally beneficial if external RAMs can be avoided.
14
--
They add to complexity of the system and bandwidth, latency
15
--
and reliability may suffer.
16
--
An internaly lookup on the other hand limits the number of
17
--
entries that can be handled. There is always a push to
18
--
support as much bandwidth as possible in the FPGAs available
19
--
and the the number of entries in the lookup tables must
20
--
often be negotiated.
21
--
Some of the lookups are simple in the sense that all bits
22
--
of the key must be matched unconditionally.
23
--
Others are more complex: Routing based on IP addresses
24
--
typically requuires longest prefix matching. Such a lookup
25
--
cannot be done using a CAM but requires a TCAM or some
26
--
(large) RAM tables.
27
--
For the simple lookups, a CAM suffices though and in many
28
--
cases the application doesn't require frequent updates of
29
--
the CAM content (say seconds apart). In this situation a
30
--
CAM function realized using perfect hashing becomes
31
--
attractive because of the low resource requirement.
32
--
33
--
34
--
Structure:
35
--
36
--
The internal hash structures are configured by a CPU.
37
--
With a perfect hash the CPU must operate on the full set of
38
--
keys when adding a single key. This takes some time and this
39
--
is why a perfect hash based CAM isn't suited for applications
40
--
where frequent updates occur.
41
--
42
--
In operation one lookup operation can be issued each clock
43
--
cycle. The result is a candidate index. The environment
44
--
must use this index to address a RAM with the keys and -
45
--
possibly - some attributes associated with the keys. Only
46
--
when the input key matches the key at the candidate position
47
--
is it known if the lookup succeeded.
48
--
49
--
It must be possible to insert entries in the CAM while traffic
50
--
is active, so the hash structures must be duplicated:
51
--
One set of structures is the active one used for the lookups
52
--
while the other set can be updated by the CPU with a new
53
--
configuration. Once the new configuration is updated and
54
--
consistent it can be made the active one.
55
--
Also it is required that the perfect hash is order-preserving:
56
--
When inserting a new entry, the indeces for the previous
57
--
entries shall not change.
58
--
59
--
It is expected that the configuration is calculated and
60
--
written by the same control plane CPU that executes the API
61
--
for the whole chip.
62
--
93
==PAGE:178==
94
APPENDIX D. APPENDIX D - FPGA IMPLEMENTATION
63
--
Later one could consider letting a small, dedicated CPU core
64
--
(like e.g. an Altera NIOS) calculate the configuration given
65
--
a set of keys. While much slower than the typical system CPU,
66
--
it is possible with e.g. a NIOS to add special, taylored
67
--
instructions. Depending on the hashing algorithm chosen, such
68
--
instructions could accelerate the processing and compensate
69
--
some for the lower speed of the CPU.
70
--
This is however a whole project in itself.
71
--
72
-- Notes:
73
-- cpu_addr_i(CPU_AW-1 downto CPU_AW-2) used as described below:
74
--
- b[13]: used to select RAM (0) or hash polynomial (1)
75
--
- b[12]: used to select between RAM0 or RAM1 or hash polynomal 0 or 1.
76
-- cpu_addr_i(CPU_AW-3 downto 0) used as described below:
77
--
- b[11:0] : physical memory addresses (4k addresses)
78
-- ##########################################################################
79
80

-- Quartus Prime VHDL Template

-- True Dual-Port RAM with single clock
3

4
-- Read-during-write on port A or B returns newly written data

6

7

8

9
-- don't care condition to optimize the performance of the RAM.
If you
10

11
-- must instantiate the altsyncram Megafunction directly.

78
component cam_perf_hash is
79
generic (
80
CPU_AW
: positive;
-- Width of CPU's word address
81
KW
: positive;
-- Width of key to look up
82
IXW
: positive;
-- Width of index returned
==PAGE:191==
D.2. TESTBENCH AND MODELSIM SETUP
107
83
DEPTH
: natural := 0;
-- The application might limit the
usable range for ix_o.
84
-- If DEPTH is zero, then the usable
range for ix_o is 0..2**IXW-1.
85
-- If DEPTH is NZ, then the usable range
for ix_o is limited to 0..DEPTH-1
86
USE_CFG_SEL : natural := 0;
-- If Z, then an internal, CPU writeable
bit selects which of two sets
87
-- of hash structures to use for lookups
.
88
-- If NZ, then the cfg_sel_i port
selects which of the two sets of
hash
89
-- structures to use. In some
applications the CPU doesn't know
exactly
90
-- when a prepared configuration shall
be activated. The change could for
91
-- example be triggered by some overhead
being received. In this case
92
-- cfg_sel_i would be used
93
FAIL_IX
: natural
-- Value output on ix_o if no match.
Might simplify processing of result
94
);
95
port (
96
-- Clock & reset
97
clk_i
: in
std_logic;
98
srst_i
: in
std_logic;
-- Synchronous reset
99
--
100
-- CPU interface for configuration of hash structures (and configuration selector if USE_CFG_SEL
= 0)
101
cpu_req_i
: in
std_logic;
-- Pulsed to request a CPU access
102
cpu_addr_i
: in
std_logic_vector(CPU_AW-1 downto 0);
-- CPU's word address. Granularity is 32
bits (byte access not supported)
103
cpu_wr_i
: in
std_logic;
-- Set if CPU access is a write. Shall
be held until access complete, i.e.
104
-- from cpu_req_i to cpu_rdy_o.
105
cpu_wrdata_i : in
std_logic_vector(32-1 downto 0);
-- CPU's write data. If a write it shall
be held until access complete
106
cpu_rdy_o
: out std_logic;
-- Pulsed when module completes a CPU
access
107
cpu_rddata_o : out std_logic_vector(32-1 downto 0);
-- Data returned to CPU. Valid if
cpu_rdy_o and a read
108
--
109
-- Operation
110
req_i
: in
std_logic;
-- Pulsed to initiate a lookup
111
cfg_sel_i
: in
std_logic := '0';
-- Only used when USE_CFG_SEL is NZ.
Then it selects the configuration
112
-- to use with the lookup. 0 if cfg#0, 1
if cfg#1. Accompanies each
113
-- lookup. I.e. it is propagated through
the lookup pipeline
114
key_i
: in
std_logic_vector(KW-1 downto 0);
-- Key to look up
115
rdy_o
: out std_logic;
-- Pulsed when lookup result presented
116
match_o
: out std_logic_vector(KW-1 downto 0);
-- Qualified by rdy_o. If set, then a
candidate ix_o was found and a
117
-- subsequent compare (outside of this
module) must determine if the key
is
118
-- actually present. This allows keys to
be stored along with their
attributes
119
ix_o
: out std_logic_vector(IXW-1 downto 0)
-- Qualified by rdy_o. Then candidate
index if match_o, FAIL_IX if not match_o
120
);
121
end component;

: integer := 16;

component cam_perf_hash is
79
generic (
80
CPU_AW
: positive;
-- Width of CPU's word address
81
KW
: positive;
-- Width of key to look up
82
IXW
: positive;
-- Width of index returned
83
DEPTH
: natural := 0;
-- The application might limit the
usable range for ix_o.
84
-- If DEPTH is zero, then the usable
range for ix_o is 0..2**IXW-1.
85
-- If DEPTH is NZ, then the usable range
for ix_o is limited to 0..DEPTH-1
86
USE_CFG_SEL : natural := 0;
-- If Z, then an internal, CPU writeable
bit selects which of two sets
87
-- of hash structures to use for lookups
.
88
-- If NZ, then the cfg_sel_i port
selects which of the two sets of
hash
89
-- structures to use. In some
applications the CPU doesn't know
exactly
90
-- when a prepared configuration shall
be activated. The change could for
91
-- example be triggered by some overhead
being received. In this case
92
-- cfg_sel_i would be used
93
FAIL_IX
: natural
-- Value output on ix_o if no match.
Might simplify processing of result
94
);
95

96
-- Clock & reset
97

: in
std_logic;
98
srst_i
: in
std_logic;
-- Synchronous reset

--
100
-- CPU interface for configuration of hash structures (and configuration selector if USE_CFG_SEL
= 0)
101
cpu_req_i
: in
std_logic;
-- Pulsed to request a CPU access
102
cpu_addr_i
: in
std_logic_vector(CPU_AW-1 downto 0);
-- CPU's word address. Granularity is 32
bits (byte access not supported)
103
cpu_wr_i
: in
std_logic;
-- Set if CPU access is a write. Shall
be held until access complete, i.e.
104
-- from cpu_req_i to cpu_rdy_o.
105
cpu_wrdata_i : in
std_logic_vector(32-1 downto 0);
-- CPU's write data. If a write it shall
be held until access complete
106
cpu_rdy_o
: out std_logic;
-- Pulsed when module completes a CPU
access
107
cpu_rddata_o : out std_logic_vector(32-1 downto 0);
-- Data returned to CPU. Valid if
cpu_rdy_o and a read
108
--
109
-- Operation
110
req_i
: in
std_logic;
-- Pulsed to initiate a lookup
111
cfg_sel_i
: in
std_logic := '0';
-- Only used when USE_CFG_SEL is NZ.
Then it selects the configuration
112
-- to use with the lookup. 0 if cfg#0, 1
if cfg#1. Accompanies each
113
-- lookup. I.e. it is propagated through
the lookup pipeline
==PAGE:209==
D.2. TESTBENCH AND MODELSIM SETUP
125
114
key_i
: in
std_logic_vector(KW-1 downto 0);
-- Key to look up
115
rdy_o
: out std_logic;
-- Pulsed when lookup result presented
116
match_o
: out std_logic_vector(KW-1 downto 0);
-- Qualified by rdy_o. If set, then a
candidate ix_o was found and a
117
-- subsequent compare (outside of this
module) must determine if the key
is
118
-- actually present. This allows keys to
be stored along with their
attributes
119
ix_o
: out std_logic_vector(IXW-1 downto 0)
-- Qualified by rdy_o. Then candidate
index if match_o, FAIL_IX if not match_o
120
);
121
end component;

input_keys : process

file Wset0_file
: text open read_mode is "W_set0_16000.csv";
612
file Wset1_file
: text open read_mode is "W_set1_16000.csv";
613
variable Wset0_row
: line;
614
variable Wset0_file_data
: std_logic_vector(KW-1 downto 0);
615
variable Wset0_file_data_cnt : integer := 0;
616
variable Wset1_row
: line;
617
variable Wset1_file_data
: std_logic_vector(KW-1 downto 0);
618

619
620
begin
621
wait until rising_edge(clk);
622
if(input_key = '1') then
623
if (cfg_sel_i = '0' and not endfile(Wset0_file)) then
624
Wset0_file_data_cnt := Wset0_file_data_cnt + 1;
625
readline(Wset0_file, Wset0_row);
626
hread(Wset0_row,Wset0_file_data);
-- read hex number from "Wset0_row" variable
627
-- Setup Key operation:
628
key_i
<= Wset0_file_data;
629
req_i
<= '1';
630
elsif (cfg_sel_i = '1' and not endfile(Wset1_file)) then
631
Wset1_file_data_cnt := Wset1_file_data_cnt + 1;
632
readline(Wset1_file, Wset1_row);
633
hread(Wset1_row,Wset1_file_data);
-- read hex number from "Wset1_row" variable
634
-- Setup Key operation:

key_i
<= Wset1_file_data;
636
req_i
<= '1';
637
else
638
key_i
<= (others => '0');
639
req_i
<= '0';
640
end if;
641
else
642
key_i
<= (others => '0');
643
req_i
<= '0';
644
end if;
